bryant 1850 to the reader the letter compose this volume be write at various time during the last sixteen year and during journey make in different country they contain however no regular account of any tour or journey make by the writer but be merely occasional sketch of what most attract he attention the greater part of they have already appear in print the author be sensible that the highest merit such a work can claim if ever so well execute be but slight he might have make these letter more interesting to reader in general if he have speak of distinguished man to whose society he be admit but the limit within which this may be do with propriety and without offense be so narrow and so easily overstep that he have prefer to abstain altogether from that class of topic he offer he book to the public with expectation which will be satisfy by a very moderate success new york _ April _ 1850 contents to the reader letter i -- first impression of a american in france -- token of impression of a american in france pari _ August _ 9 1834 since we first land in france every step of we journey have remind we that we be in a old country every thing we see speak of the past of a antiquity without limit everywhere we eye rest on the handiwork of those who have be dead for age and we be in the midst of custom which they have bequeath to they descendant the church be so vast so solid so venerable and time-eaten the dwelling so gray and of such antique architecture and in the large town like rouen rise so high and overhung with such quaint projection the narrow and cavernous street the thatched cot be so mossy and so green with grass the very hill about they look scarcely as old for there be youth in they vegetation -- they shrub and flower the countrywoman wear such high cap such long waist and such short petticoat -- the fashion of bonnet be a innovation of yesterday which they regard with scorn we pass female ride on donkey the old testament beast of burden with pannier on each side as be the custom hundred of year since we see ancient dame sit at they door with distaff twist the thread by twirl the spindle between the thumb and finger as they do in the day of homer a flock of sheep be grazing on the side of a hill they be attend by a shepherd and a brace of prick-eared dog which keep they from stray as be do thousand of year ago speckled bird be hop by the side of the road it be the magpie the bird of ancient fable flock of what i at first take for the crow of we country be stalk in the field or sailing in the air over the old elm it be the rook the bird make as classical by addison as he cousin the raven by the latin poet then there be the old chateaus on the hill build with a appearance of military strength they tower and battlements tell of feudal time the grove by which they be surround be for the most part clip into regular wall and pierce with regularly arched passage lead in various direction and the tree compel by the shears to take the shape of obelisk and pyramid or other fantastic figure accord to the taste of the middle age as we draw nearer to pari we see the plant which noah first commit to the earth after the deluge -- you know what that be i hope -- train on low stake and grow thickly and luxuriantly on the slope by the side of the highway here too be the tree which be the subject of the first christian miracle the fig its branch heavy with the burst fruit just begin to ripen for the market but when we enter pari and pass the barrière d'etoile with its lofty triumphal arch when we sweep through the arch of neuilly and come in front of the hotel des invalides where the aged or maimed soldier the living monument of so many battle be walk or sit under the elm of its broad esplanade when we see the colossal statue of statesman and warrior frown from they pedestal on the bridge which bestride the muddy and narrow channel of the seine when we come in sight of the gray pinnacle of the tuillery and the gothic tower of notre-dame and the roman one of st sulpice and the dome of the pantheon under which lie the remains of so many of the great man of france and the dark column of place vendòme work with figure in relief and the obelisk bring from egypt to ornament the place loui quatorze the association with antiquity which the country present from be general become particular and historical they be recollection of power and magnificence and extend empire of valor and skill in war which have hold the world in fear of dynasty that have rise and pass away of battle and victory which have leave no other fruit than they monument the solemnity of these recollection do not seem to press with much weight upon the mind of the people it have be say that the french have become a graver nation than formerly if so what must have be they gayety a hundred year ago to I they seem as light-hearted and as easily amuse as if they have do nothing but make love and quiz they priest since the day of loui xiv -- as if they street have never flow with the blood of frenchman shed by they brethren -- as if they have never win and lose a mighty empire i can not imagine the present generation to be less gay than that which listen to the comedy of molière at they first representation particularly when i perceive that even molière 's piece be too much burden with thought for a frenchman of the present day and that he prefer the lighter and more frivolous vaudeville the parisian have he amusement as regularly as he meal the theater music the dance a walk in the tuillery a refection in the café to which lady resort as commonly as the other sex perpetual business perpetual labor be a thing of which he seem to have no idea i wake in the middle of the night and i hear the fiddle go and the sound of foot keep time in some of the dependency of the large building near the tuillery in which i have my lodgings when a generation of frenchman have play and laugh and dance and drink they fill -- when they have see they allotted number of vaudeville and swallow they destine allowance of weak wine and bottled small-beer they be sweep off to the cemetery of montmartre or of père la chaise or some other of the great burial-place which lie just without the city i go to visit the latter of these the other day you be remind of you approach to it by the row of stone-cutter ' shop on each side of the street with a glitter display of polished marble monument the place of the dead be almost a gayer-looking spot than the ordinary haunt of parisian life it be traverse with shady walk of elm and lime and its inmate lie amidst thicket of ornamental shrub and plantation of the most gaudy flower they monument be hang with wreath of artificial flower or of those natural one which do not lose they color and shape in dry like the amaranth and the ever-lasting part of the cemetery seem like a city in miniature the sepulchral chapel through the window of which you see crucifix and taper stand close to each other beside the path intermingle with statue and bust there be one part of this repository of the dead which be little visit that in which the poor be bury where those who have dwell apart from they more fortunate fellow-creature in life lie apart in death here be no walk no shade of tree no plant shrubbery but ridge of raw earth and tuft of coarse herbage show where the body be throw together under a thin covering of soil i be about to walk over the spot but be repel by the sickening exhalation that rise from it letter ii a journey to florence florence _ sept _ 27 1834 i have now be in this city a fortnight and have establish myself in a suite of apartment lately occupy as the landlord tell I in hope i presume of get a higher rent by a russian prince the arno flow or rather stand still under my window for the water be low and near the western wall of the city be frugally dam up to preserve it for the public bath beyond this stream so renowned in history and poetry be at this season but a feeble rill almost lose among the pebble of its bed and scarcely suffice to give drink to the pheasant and hare of the grand duke 's cascine on its bank opposite my lodgings at the south end of the _ ponte alla carraia _ be a little oratory before the door of which every good catholic who pass take off he hat with a gesture of homage and at this moment a swarthy weasel-faced man with a tin box in he hand be gather contribution to pay for the service of the chapel rattle he coin to attract the attention of the pedestrian and call out to those who seem disposed to pass without pay to the north and west the peak of the appenine be in full sight rise over the spire of the city and the grove of the cascine every evening i see they through the soft delicately-colored haze of a italian sunset look as if they have catch something of the transparency of the sky and appear like mountain of fairy-land instead of the bleak and barren ridge of rock which they really be the weather since my arrival in tuscany have be continually serene the sky wholly cloudless and the temperature uniform -- oppressively warm in the street at noon delightful at morning and evening with a long beautiful golden twilight occasion by the reflection of light from the orange-colored haze which invest the atmosphere every night i be remind that i be in the land of song for until two o'clock in the morning i hear all manner of tune chant by people in the street in all manner of voice i believe i have give you no account of we journey from pari to this place that part of it which lay between paris and chalon on the saone may be describe in a very few word monotonous plain cover with vineyard and wheat-field with very few tree and those spoil by be lop for fuel -- sunburnt woman drive cart or at work in the field -- gloomy cheerless-looking town with narrow filthy street -- troops of beggar surround you carriage whenever you stop or whenever the nature of the road oblige the horse to walk and chant they request in the most doleful whine imaginable -- such be the sight and sound that meet you for the greater part of two hundred and fifty mile there be however some exception as to the aspect of the country autun one of the most ancient town of france and yet retain some remains of roman architecture lie in a beautiful and picturesque region a little beyond that town we ascend a hill by a road wind along a glen the rocky side of which be clothe with a unpruned wood and a clear stream run dash over the stone now on one side of the road and then on the other -- the first instance of a brook leave to follow its natural channel which i have see in france two young frenchman who be we fellow-passenger be wild with delight at this glimpse of unspoiled nature they follow the meanderings of the stream leap from rock to rock and shout till the wood ring again of chalon i have nothing to tell you abelard die there and he tomb be erect with that of eloise in the church of st marcel but the church be destroy and the monument have be transport to the cemetery of père la chaise and with it all the poetry of the place be vanish but if you would make yourself supremely uncomfortable travel as i do in a steamboat down the saone from chalon to lyon on a rainy day crowd into a narrow dirty cabin with bench on each side and a long table in the middle at which a set of frenchman with they hat on be play card and eat _ déjeuners à la fourchette _ all day long and deafen you with they noise while waiter be run against you leg and tread on you toe every moment and the water be drop on you head through the crack of the deck-floor you would be force to admit the superlative misery of such a mode of travel the approach to lyon however make some amends for these inconvenience the shore of the river hitherto low and level begin to rise into hill break with precipice and crown by castle some in ruin and other entire and seemingly a part of the very rock on which they stand so old and mossy and strong do they seem what strike I most in lyon be the superiority of its people in look and feature to the inhabitant of paris -- the clatter and jar of silk-loom with which its street resound -- and the picturesque beauty of its situation place as it be among steep and rock with the quiet saone on one side and the swiftly-running rhone on the other in we journey from lyon to marseille we travel by land instead of take the steamboat as be commonly do as far as avignon the common book of travels will tell you how numerous be the ruin of feudal time perch upon the height all along the rhone remnant of fortress and castle overlook a vast extent of country and once serve as place of refuge to the cultivator of the soil who dwell in they vicinity -- how frequently also be to be meet with the earlier yet scarcely less fresh trace of roman colonization and dominion in gateway triumphal arch wall and monument -- how on enter provence you find yourself among a people of a different physiognomy from those of the northern province speak a language which rather resemble italian than french -- how the beauty of the woman of avignon still do credit to the taste of the clergy who make that city for more than half a century the seat of the papal power -- and how as you approach the shore of the mediterranean the mountain which rise from the fruitful valley shoot up in wilder form until they summit become mere pinnacle of rock wholly bare of vegetation marseille be seat in the midst of a semicircle of mountain of whitish rock the steep and naked side of which scarce afford a footing for the goat stretch into the mediterranean they inclose a commodious harbor in front of which be two or three rocky island anchor in a sea of more vivid blue than any water i have ever before see the country immediately surround the city be a arid and dusty valley intersect here and there with the bed of a brook or torrent dry during the summer it be carefully cultivate however and plant with vineyard and orchard of olive fig and pomegranate tree the tree be small and low the foliage of the olive thin and pale the leaf of the fig broad and few and the soil appear everywhere at they root as well as between the row of vine the vegetation when view from a little distance have a meager and ragged appearance the whiteness of the hill which the eye can hardly bear to rest upon at noon the intense blue of the sea the peculiar form of the foliage and the deficiency of shade and verdure make I almost fancy myself in a tropical region the greek judge well of the commercial advantage of marseille when they make it the seat of one of they early colony i find its street animated with a bustle which i have not see since i leave new york and its port throng with vessel from all the nation whose coast border upon the great midland sea of europe marseille be the most flourishing seaport in france it have already become to the mediterranean what new york be to the united state and its trade be regularly increase the old town be ugly but the lower or new part be nobly build of the light-colored stone so commonly use in france and so easily work -- with broad street and what be rare in french town convenient sidewalk new street be lay out garden be convert into building-lot the process of level hill and fill up hollow be go on as in new york the city be extend itself on every side and large fortune have be make by the rise in the value of land property in a conversation with a intelligent gentleman resident at marseille and largely engage in commercial and moneyed transaction the subject of the united state bank be mention opinion in france on this question of we domestic politics differ accord as the opportunity of information possess by the individual be more or less ample or as he be more or less in favor of chartered bank the gentleman remark that without any reference to the question of the united state bank he hope the day would never come when such a institution would be establish in france the project he say have some advocate but they have not yet succeed and he hope never would succeed in the introduction of that system of paper currency which prevail in the united state he deprecate the dangerous and uncertain facility of obtain credit which be the fruit of that system which produce the most ruinous fluctuation in commerce encourage speculation and extravagance of all kind and involve the prudent and laborious in the ruin which fall upon the rash and reckless he declare himself satisfied with the state of the currency of france with which if fortune be not suddenly build up they be not suddenly overthrow and period of apparent prosperity be not follow by season of real distress i make the journey from marseille to florence by land how grand and wild be the mountain that overlook the mediterranean how intense be the heat as we wind we way along the gallery of rock cut to form a road how excellent be the fruit and how thick the mosquito at nice how sumptuous be the palace how narrow and dark the street and how pallid the dame of genoa and how beautiful we find we path among the tree overrun with vine as we approach southern italy be matter which i will take some other opportunity of relate on the 12th of September we _ vetturino _ set we down safe at the _ hotel de l'europe _ in florence i think i shall return to america even a better patriot than when i leave it a citizen of the united state travel on the continent of europe find the contrast between a government of power and a government of opinion force upon he at every step he find himself delay at every large town and at every frontier of a kingdom or principality to submit to a strict examination of the passport with which the jealousy of the ruler of these country have compel he to furnish himself he see everywhere guard and sentinel arm to the tooth station in the midst of a population engage in they ordinary occupation in a time of profound peace and to supply the place of the young and robust thus withdraw from the labor of agriculture he behold woman perform the work of the field he see the many retain in a state of hopeless dependence and poverty the effect of institution forge by the ruling class to accumulate wealth in they own hand the want of self-respect in the inferior class engender by this state of thing show itself in the act of rapacity and fraud which the traveller meet with throughout france and italy and worse still in the shameless corruption of the italian custom-house the officer of which regularly solicit a paltry bribe from every passenger as the consideration of leave he baggage unexamined i be tell that in this place the custom of give present extend even to the court of justice the officer of which from the highest to the lowest be in the constant practice of receive they no american can see how much jealousy and force on the one hand and necessity and fear on the other have to do with keep up the exist government of europe without thank heaven that such be not the condition of he own country letter iii tuscan scenery and climate florence _ October _ 11 1834 the bridge over the arno immediately under my window be the spot from which cole 's fine landscape which you perhaps remember see in the exhibition of we academy be take it give you may recollect a view of the arno travel off towards the west its bank overhang with tree the mountain-ridge rise in the distance and above they the sky flush with the color of sunset the same rich hue i behold every evening in the quarter where they be see by the artist when he make they permanent on he canvas there be a great deal of prattle about italian sky : the sky and cloud of italy so far as i have have a opportunity of judge do not present so great a variety of beautiful appearance as we own but the italian atmosphere be far more uniformly fine than ours not to speak of its astonishing clearness it be pervade by a certain warmth of color which enrich every object this be more remarkable about the time of sunset when the mountain put on a aerial aspect as if they belong to another and fairer world and a little after the sun have go down the air be flush with a glory which seem to transfigure all that it inclose many of the fine old palace of florence you know be build in a gloomy though grand style of architecture of a dark-colored stone massive and lofty and overlook narrow street that lie in almost perpetual shade but at the hour of which i be speak the bright warm radiance reflect from the sky to the earth fill the darkest lane stream into the most shadowy nook and make the prison-like structure glitter as with a brightness of they own it be now nearly the middle of October and we have have no frost the strong summer heat which prevail when i come hither have by the slowest gradation subside into a agreeable autumnal temperature the tree keep they verdure but i perceive they foliage grow thinner and when i walk in the cascine on the other side of the arno the rustling of the lizard as they run among the heap of crisp leaf remind I that the autumn be wear away though the ivy which clothes the old elm have put forth a profuse array of blossom and the walk murmur with bee like we orchard in spring as i look along the declivity of the appenine i see the raw earth every day more visible between the rank of olive-tree and the well-pruned maple which support the vine if i have find my expectation of italian scenery in some respects below the reality in other respects they have be disappoint the form of the mountain be wonderfully picturesque and they effect be heighten by the rich atmosphere through which they be see and by the building impose from they architecture or venerable from time which crown the eminence but if the hand of man have do something to embellish this region it have do more to deform it not a tree be suffer to retain its natural shape not a brook to flow in its natural channel a exterminate war be carry on against the natural herbage of the soil the country be without wood and green field and to he who view the vale of the arno from the top of fiesole or any of the neighbor height grand as he will allow the circle of the mountain to be and magnificent the edifice with which the region be adorn it appear at any time after midsummer a huge valley of dust plant with low row of the pallid and thin-leaved olive or the more dwarfish maple on which the vine be train the simplicity of nature so far as can be do be destroy there be no fine sweep of forest no broad expanse of meadow or pasture ground no ancient and towering tree cluster about the villa no row of natural shrubbery follow the course of the brook and river the stream which be often but the bed of torrent dry during the summer be confine in straight channel by stone wall and embankment the slope be break up and disfigure by terrace and the tree be keep down by constant pruning and lop until half way up the side of the appenine where the limit of cultivation be reach and thence to the summit be a barren steep of rock without herbage or soil the grander feature of the landscape however be fortunately beyond the power of man to injure the lofty mountain-summits bare precipice cleft with chasm and pinnacle of rock pierce the sky betoken far more than any thing i have see elsewhere a break up of the crust of the globe in some early period of its existence i be tell that in may and June the country be much more beautiful than at present and that owe to a drought it now appear under a particular disadvantage the academy of the fine art have have its exhibition since i arrive in its room which be gratuitously open to the public i find a large crowd of gazer at the picture and statue many have come to look at some work order by a acquaintance other make the place a morning lounge in the collection be some landscape by morghen the son of the celebrated engraver very fresh and clear a few piece send by bezzolus one of the most eminent italian painter of he time a statue of galileo not without merit by costolus for there be always a galileo or two i believe at every exhibition of the kind in florence portrait good bad and indifferent in great abundance which realize the old mythological fable of beauty bear of the sea i must confess however that my first feeling on enter it be that of disappointment as we pass in we gondola out of the lagoon up one of the numerous canal which permeate the city in every direction in such a manner that it seem as if you could only pass you time either within door or in a boat the place appear to I a vast assemblage of prison surround with they moat and i think how weary i should soon grow of my island prison and how glad to escape again to the main-land but this feeling quickly give way to delight and admiration when i land and survey the clean though narrow street never incommode by dust nor disturbed by the noise and jostle of carriage and horse by which you may pass to every part of the city -- when i look again at the row of superb building with they marble step ascend out of the water of the canal in which the gondola be shooting by each other -- when i stand in the immense square of st mark surround by palace rest on arcade under which the shop rival in splendor those of pari and crowd of the gay inhabitant of both sex assemble towards evening and sit in group before the door of the coffee-house -- and when i gaze on the barbaric magnificence of the church of st mark and the doge 's palace surround by the old emblem of the power of venice and overlook the adriatic once the empire of the republic the architecture of venice have to my eye something watery and oceanic in its aspect under the hand of palladio the grecian order seem to borrow the lightness and airiness of the gothic as you look at the numerous window and the multitude of column which give a striated appearance to the front of the palace you think of stalactite and icicle such as you might imagine to ornament the abode of the water-god and sea-nymph the only thing need to complete the poetic illusion be transparency or brilliancy of color and this be wholly want for at venice the whitest marble be soon clouded and blacken by the corrosion of the sea-air it be not my intention however to do so hackneyed a thing as to give a description of venice one thing i must confess seem to I extraordinary : how this city deprive as it be of the commerce which build it up from the shallows of the adriatic and uphold it so long and so proudly should not have decay even more rapidly than it have do trieste have draw from it almost all its trade and flourish by its decline i walk through the arsenal of venice which comprehend the navy yard a enormous structure with range of broad lofty roof support by massive portion of wall and spacious dock-yard the whole large enough to build and fit out a navy for the british empire the pleasure-boat of napoleon and he empress and that of the present viceroy be there : but the ship of war belong to the republic have moulder away with the bucentaur i see however two austrian vessel the same which have convey the polish exile to new york lie under shelter in the dock as if place there to show who be the present master of the place it be melancholy to wander through the vast unoccupied space of this noble edifice and to think what must have be the riches the power the prosperity and the hope of venice at the time it be build and what they be at the present moment it seem almost impossible that any thing should take place to arrest the ruin which be gradually consume this renowned city some writer have assert that the lagoon around it be annually grow shallower by the deposition of earth bring down by stream from the land that they must finally become marsh and that they consequent insalubrity will drive the inhabitant from venice i do not know how this may be but the other cause i have mention seem likely to produce nearly the same effect i remember as these idea pass through my mind a passage in which one of the sacred poet foretell the desertion and desolation of tyre the city that make itself glorious in the midst of the sea thy riches and thy fair thy merchandise thy mariner and thy pilot thy calker and the occupier of thy merchandise and all thy man of war that be in thee shall fall into the midst of the sea in the day of thy ruin i leave this most pleasing of the italian city which i have see on the 24th of June and take the road for the tyrol we pass through a level fertile country formerly the territory of venice plain bath by the isar in which munich be situate letter vii a excursion to rock river princeton illinoi _ June _ 21 1841 i have just return from a excursion to rock river one of the most beautiful of they pursuer and destroyer not the settler of the region for they do not shoot often except at a deer or a wild turkey or a noxious animal but the prairie-hawk the bald-eagle the mink and the prairie-wolf which make merciless havoc among they and they brood about fifteen mile we come to dad joe 's grove in the shadow of which thirteen year ago a settler name joe smith who have fight in the battle of the thame one of the first white inhabitant of this region seat himself and plant he corn and gather he crop quietly through the whole indian war without be molest by the savage though he be careful to lead he wife and family to a place of security as smith be a settler of such long standing he be look to as a kind of patriarch in the county and to distinguish he from other joe smith he receive the venerable appellation of dad he have since remove to another part of the state but he well-known hospitable cabin inhabit by another inmate be still there and he grove of tall tree stand on a ridge amidst the immense savannah yet retain he name as we descend into the prairie we be strike with the novelty and beauty of the prospect which lay before we the ground sink gradually and gently into a low but immense basin in the midst of which lie the marshy tract call the winnebago swamp to the northeast the sight be intercept by a forest in the midst of the basin but to the northwest the prairie be see swell up again in the smoothest slope to they usual height and stretch away to a distance so vast that it seem boldness in the eye to follow they the winnebago and other indian tribe which formerly possess this country have leave few memorial of they existence except the name of place now and then as at indiantown near princeton you be show the hole in the ground where they store they maize and sometimes on the border of the river you see the trunk of tree which they fell evidently hack by they tomahawk but perhaps the most remarkable of they remains be the path across the prairie or beside the large stream call indian trail -- narrow and well-beaten way sometimes a foot in depth and many of they doubtless trodden for hundred of year as we go down the ridge upon which stand dad joe 's grove we see many boulder of rock lie on the surface of the soil of the prairie the western people naturally puzzle to tell how they come there give they the expressive name of lose rock we enter a forest of scatter oak and after travel for half a hour reach the winnebago swamp a tract cover with tall and luxuriant water-grass which we cross on a causey build by a settler who keep a toll-gate and at the end of the causey we ford a small stream call winnebago inlet cross another vast prairie we reach the neighborhood of dixon the approach to which be denote by grove farm-house herd of cattle and inclosed corn field checker the broad green prairie dixon name after a ancient settler of the place still live be a country town situate on a high bank of rock river five year ago two log-cabin only stand on the solitary shore and now it be a considerable village with many neat dwelling a commodious court-house several place of worship for the good people and a jail for the rogue build with a triple wall of massive log but i be glad to see that it have no inmate rock river flow through high prairie and not like most stream of the west through a alluvial country the current be rapid and the pellucid water glide over a bottom of sand and pebble its admirer declare that its shore unite the beauty of the hudson and of the connecticut the bank on either side be high and bold sometimes they be perpendicular precipice the base of which stand in the run water sometimes they be steep grassy or rocky bluff with a space of dry alluvial land between they and the stream sometimes they rise by a gradual and easy ascent to the general level of the region and sometimes this ascent be interrupt by a broad natural terrace majestic tree grow solitary or in clump on the grassy acclivity or scatter in natural park along the lower land upon the river or in thick grove along the edge of the high country back of the bluff extend a fine agricultural region rich prairie with a undulating surface intersperse with grove at the foot of the bluff break forth copious spring of clear water which hasten in little brook to the river in a drive which i take up the leave bank of the river i see three of these in the space of as many mile one of these be the spring which supply the town of dixon with water the next be a beautiful fountain rush out from the rock in the midst of a clump of tree as merrily and in as great a hurry as a boy let out of school the third be so remarkable as to have receive a name it be a little rivulet issue from a cavern six or seven foot high and about twenty from the entrance to the further end at the foot of a perpendicular precipice cover with forest-tree and fringed with bush in the neighborhood of dixon a class of emigrant have establish themselves more opulent and more luxurious in they taste than most of the settler of the western country some of these have build elegant mansion on the left bank of the river amidst the noble tree which seem to have grow up for that very purpose indeed when i look at they i could hardly persuade myself that they have not be plant to overshadow older habitation from the door of one of these dwelling i survey a prospect of exceed beauty the winding of the river allow we a sight of its water and its beautifully diversified bank to a great distance each way and in one direction a high prairie region be see above the wood that fringe the course of this river of a lighter green than they and touch with the golden light of the setting sun i be tell that the character of rock river be throughout its course much as i have describe it in the neighborhood of dixon that its bank be high and free from marsh and its water rapid and clear from its source in wisconsin to where it enter the mississippus amidst rocky island what should make its shore unhealthy i can not see yet they who inhabit they be much subject to intermittent fever they tell you very quietly that every body who come to live there must take a seasoning i suppose that when this country become settle this will no longer be the case rock river be not much subject to inundation nor do its water become very low in summer a project be on foot i be tell to navigate it with steam-vessel of a light draught when i arrive at dixon i be tell that the day before a man name bridge living at washington grove in ogle county come into town and complain that he have receive notice from a certain association that he must leave the county before the seventeenth of the month or that he would be look upon as a proper subject for lynch law he ask for assistance to defend he person and dwelling against the lawless violence of these man the people of dixon county come together and pass a resolution to the effect that they approve fully of what the inhabitant of ogle county have do and that they allow mr bridge the term of four hour to depart from the town of dixon he go away immediately and in great trepidation this bridge be a notorious confederate and harborer of horse-thief and counterfeiter the thinly-settled portion of illinoi be much expose to the depredation of horse-thief who have a kind of center of operation in ogle county where it be say that they have a justice of the peace and a constable among they own associate and where they contrive to secure a friend on the jury whenever any one of they number be try trial after trial have take place and it have be find impossible to obtain a conviction on the clearest evidence until last April when two horse-thief be on trial eleven of the jury threaten the twelfth with a taste of the cowskin unless he would bring in a verdict of guilty he do so and the man be condemn before they be remove to the state-prison the court-house be burn down and the jail be in flame but luckily they be extinguish without the liberation of the prisoner such at length become the general feeling of insecurity that three hundred citizen of ogle county as i understand have form themselves into a company of volunteer for the purpose of clear the county of these man two horse-thief have be seize and flog and bridge they patron have be order to remove or abide the consequence as we be return from dixon on the morning of the 19th we hear a kind of hum noise in the grass which one of the company say proceed from a rattlesnake we dismount and find in fact it be make by a prairie-rattlesnake which lay coil around a tuft of herbage and which we soon dispatch the indian call this small variety of the rattlesnake the massasauger horse be frequently bite by it and come to the door of they owner with they head horribly swell but they be recover by the application of hartshorn a little further on one of the party raise the cry of wolf and look we see a prairie-wolf in the path before we a prick-eared animal of a reddish-gray color standing and gaze at we with great composure as we approach he trot off into the grass with he nose near the ground not deign to hasten he pace for we shout and shortly afterward we see two other run in a different direction the prairie-wolf be not so formidable a animal as the name of wolf would seem to denote he be quite as great a coward as robber but he be exceedingly mischievous he never take full-grown sheep unless he go with a strong troop of he friend but seize young lamb carry off sucking-pig rob the henroost devour sweet corn in the garden and plunder the water-melon patch a herd of prairie-wolf will enter a field of melon and quarrel about the division of the spoil as fiercely and noisily as so many politician it be they way to gnaw a hole immediately into the first melon they lay hold of if it happen to be ripe the inside be devour at once if not it be drop and another be seek out and a quarrel be pick with the discoverer of a ripe one and loud and shrill be the bark and fierce the growl and snap which be hear on these occasion it be surprising i be tell with what dexterity a wolf will make the most of a melon absorb every remnant of the pulp and hollow it out as clean as it could be scrape by a spoon this be when the allowance of melon be scarce but when they be abundant he be as careless and wasteful as a government agent enough of natural history i will finish my letter another day _ June 26th _ let I caution all emigrant to illinois not to handle too familiarly the wild parsnip as it be commonly call a umbelliferous plant grow in the moist prairie of this region i have handle it and have pay dearly for it have such a swell face that i could scarcely see for several day the regulator of ogle county remove bridge 's family on Monday last and demolish he house he make preparation to defend himself and keep twenty armed man about he for two day but think at last that the regulator do not mean to carry they threat into effect he dismiss they he have take refuge with he friend the aikin family who live i believe in jefferson grove in the same county and who it be say have also receive notice to quit letter viii example of lynch law princeton illinoi _ July 2 1841 _ in my last letter i mention that the regulator in ogle county on rock river in this state have pull down the house of one bridge living at washington grove a well-known confederate of the horse-thief and coiner with which this region be infested horse-thief be numerous in this part of the country a great number of horse be breed here you see large herd of they feed in the open prairie and at this season of the year every full-grown mare have a colt run by she side most of the theft be commit early in the spring when the grass begin to shoot and the horse be turn out on the prairie and the thief have have little or no employment during the winter be needy or else in the autumn when the animal be keep near the dwelling of they owner to be feed with indian corn and be in excellent order the thief select the best from the drive and these be pass from one station to another till they arrive at some distant market where they be sell it be say that they have they regular line of communication from wisconsin to st loui and from the wabash to the mississippus in ogle county they seem to have be bolder than elsewhere and more successful notwithstanding the notoriety of they crime in avoid punishment the impossibility of punish they by process of law the burning of the court-house at oregon city last April and the threat of deadly vengeance throw out by they against such as should attempt to bring they to justice lead to the formation of a company of citizen regulator they call themselves who resolve to take the law into they own hand and drive the felon from the neighborhood this be not the first instance of the kind which have happen in illinois some twenty year since the southern county contain a gang of horse-thief so numerous and well-organized as to defy punishment by legal means and they be expel by the same method which be now adopt in ogle county i have just learn since i write the last sentence that the society of regulator include not only the county of ogle but those of de kalb and winnebago where the depredation of the horse-thief and the perfect impunity with which they manage to exercise they call have exhaust the patience of the inhabitant in those county as well as in ogle they patron live at some of the finest grove where they own large farm ten or twenty steal horse will be bring to one of these place of a night and before sunrise the desperado employ to take they be again mount and on they way to some other station in break up these haunt the regulator i understand have proceed with some of the formality commonly use in administer justice the accuse party have be allow to make he defense and witness have be examine both for and against he these proceedings however have lately suffer a most tragical interruption not long after bridge 's house be pull down two man mount and carry rifle call at the dwelling of a mr campbell living at whiterock grove in ogle county who belong to the company of regulator and who have act as the messenger to convey to bridge the order to leave the county meeting mr campbell without the house they tell she that they wish to speak to she husband campbell make he appearance at the door and immediately both the man fire he fall mortally wound and live but a few minute you have kill my husband say mr campbell to one of the murderer whose name be driscoll upon this they ride off at full speed as soon as the event be know the whole country be rouse and every man who be not a associate of the horse-thief shoulder he rifle to go in pursuit of the murderer they apprehend the father of driscoll a man nearly seventy year of age and one of he son william driscoll the former a reputed horse-thief and the latter a man who have hitherto bear a tolerably fair character and subject they to a separate examination the father be wary in he answer and put on the appearance of perfect innocence but william driscoll be greatly agitate and confess that he with he father and other have plan the murder of campbell and that david driscoll he brother together with another associate be employ to execute it the father and son be then sentence to death they be bind and make to kneel about fifty man take aim at each and in three hour from the time they be take they be dead man a pit be dig on the spot where they fall in the midst of a prairie near they dwelling they corpse pierce with bullet-hole in every part be throw in and the earth be heap over they the pursuit of david driscoll and the fellow who be with he when campbell be kill be still go on with great activity more than a hundred man be traverse the country in different direction determine that no lurking-place shall hide they in the mean time various person who have the reputation of be confederate of horse-thief not only in ogle county but in the adjoining one even in this have receive can not learn letter ix richmond in virginium richmond virginium _ march 2 1843 _ i arrive at this place last night from washington where i have observe little worth describe the statue of we first president by greenough be of course one of the thing which i take a early opportunity of look at and although the bad light in which it be place prevent the spectator from properly appreciate the feature i could not help see with satisfaction that no position however unfavorable could impair the majesty of that noble work or at all event destroy its grand general effect the house of representative i have not see since 1832 and i perceive that the proceedings be conduct with less apparent decorum than formerly and that the member no longer sit with they hat on whether they have come to the conclusion that it be well to sit uncovered in order to make up by this token of mutual respect for the too frequent want of decorum in they proceedings or whether the change have be make because it so often happen that all the member be talk together the rule be that the person speak must be barehead or whether finally it be find during the late long summer session that a hat make the wearer really uncomfortable be question which i ask on the spot but to which i get no satisfactory answer i visit the senate chamber and see a member of that dignified body as somebody call it in prepare to make a speech blow he nose with he thumb and finger without the intervention of a pocket-handkerchief the speech after this graceful preliminary do not i confess disappoint I whoever go to washington should by all means see the museum at the patent office enrich by the collection lately bring back by the expedition send out to explore the pacific i be surprise at the extent and variety of these collection dress weapon and domestic implement of savage nation in such abundance as to leave one would almost think they little tribe disfurnished bird of strange shape and plumage fish of remote water whole grove of different kind of coral sea-shell of rare form and singular beauty from the most distant shore mummy from the cave of peru curious mineral and plant : whoever be interested by such object as these should give the museum a more leisurely examination than i have time to do the person engage in arrange and put up these collection be still at they task when i be at washington and i learn that what i saw be by no mean the whole the night before we set out snow fall to the depth of three inch and as the steamboat pass down the potomac we see at sunrise the grounds of mount vernon lie in a covering of the purest white the snow scatter in patch on the thick foliage of cedar that skirt the river look like cluster of blossom about twelve the steamboat come to land and the railway take we through a gorge of the woody hill that skirt the potomac in about a hour we be at fredericksburg on the rappahannock the day be bright and cold and the wind keen and cut a crowd of negro come about the car with cake fruit and other refreshment the poor fellow seem collapse with the unusual cold they face and lip be of the color which draper call blue-black as we proceed southward in virginium the snow gradually become thinner and finally disappear altogether it be impossible to mistake the region in which we be broad inclosure be around we with sign of extensive and superficial cultivation large dwelling be see at a distance from each other and each with its group of smaller building look as solitary and chilly as french chateaus and now and then we see a gang of negro at work in the field though oftener we pass mile without the sight of a living creature at six in the afternoon we arrive at richmond a beautiful city be richmond seat on the hill that overlook the jame river the dwelling have a pleasant appearance often stand by themselves in the midst of garden in front of several i see large magnolia they dark glazed leaf glitter in the march sunshine the river as yellow as the tiber its water now stain with the earth of the upper country run by the upper part of the town in noisy rapid embrace several island shaded with the plane-tree the hackberry and the elm and prolific in spring and summer of wild-flower i go upon one of these island by means of a foot-bridge and be point to another the resort of a quoit-club comprise some of the most distinguished man of richmond among whom in he lifetime be judge marshall who sometimes join in this athletic sport we descend one of the hill on which the town be build and go up another to the east where stand a ancient house of religious worship the oldest episcopal church in the state it be in the midst of a burying-ground where sleep some of the founder of the colony whose old grave be greenly overgrow with the trail and mat periwinkle in this church patrick henry at the commencement of the american revolution make that celebrated speech which so vehemently move all who hear he end with the sentence : give I liberty or give I death we look in at one of the window it be a low plain room with small square pew and a sound board over the little pulpit from the hill on which this church stand you have a beautiful view of the surrounding country a gently undulate surface close in by hill on the west and the jame river be see wander through it by distant plantation and between border of tree a place be point out to we a little way down the river which bear the name of powhatan and here i be tell a flat rock be still show as the one on which captain smith be place by he captor in order to be put to death when the intercession of pocahonta save he life i go with a acquaintance to see the inspection and sale of tobacco huge upright column of dry leaf firmly pack and of a greenish hue stand in row under the roof of a broad low building open on all side -- these be the hogshead of tobacco strip of the stave the inspector a portly man with a bourbon face he white hair gather in a tie behind go very quietly and expeditiously through he task of determine the quality after which the vast bulk be dispose of in a very short time with surprisingly little noise to the tobacco merchant tobacco to the value of three million of dollar annually be send by the planter to richmond and thence distribute to different nation whose merchant frequent this mart in the sale it be always sure to bring cash which to those who detest the weed be a little difficult to understand i go afterwards to a tobacco factory the sight of which amuse I though the narcotic fume make I cough in one room a black man be take apart the small bundle of leaf of which a hogshead of tobacco be compose and carefully separate leaf from leaf other be assort the leaf accord to the quality and other again be arrange the leaf in layer and sprinkle each layer with the extract of liquorice in another room be about eighty negro boy they be call from the age of twelve year up to manhood who receive the leaf thus prepared roll they into long even roll and then cut they into plug of about four inch in length which be afterwards pass through a press and thus become ready for market as we enter the room we hear a murmur of psalmody run through the sable assembly which now and then swell into a strain of very tolerable music verse sweeten toil -- say the stanza which dr johnson be so fond of quote and really it be so good that i will transcribe the whole of it -- verse sweeten toil however rude the sound -- all at she work the village maiden sing nor while she turn the giddy wheel around revolve the sad vicissitude of thing verse it seem can sweeten the toil of slave in a tobacco factory we encourage they singing as much as we can say the brother of the proprietor himself a diligent masticator of the weed who attend we and politely explain to we the process of make plug tobacco we encourage it as much as we can for the boy work better while singing sometimes they will sing all day long with great spirit at other time you will not hear a single note they must sing wholly of they own accord it be of no use to bid they do it what be remarkable he continue they tune be all psalm tune and the word be from hymn-book they taste be exclusively for sacred music they will sing nothing else almost all these person be church-member we have not a dozen about the factory who be not so most of they be of the baptist persuasion a few be methodist i saw in the course of the day the baptist church in which these people worship a low plain but spacious brick build the same in which the sage of virginium a generation of great man debate the provision of the constitution it have a congregation of twenty-seven hundred person and the best choir i hear somebody say in all richmond near it be the monumental church erect on the site of the richmond theater after the terrible fire which carry mourning into so many family in pass through a old part of main-street i be show a ancient stone cottage of rude architecture and humble dimension which be once the best hotel in richmond here i be tell there be those in richmond who remember dine with general washington judge marshall and they cotemporary i could not help compare it with the palace-like building put up at richmond within two or three year past name the exchange hotel with its spacious parlor its long dining-room its airy dormitory and its ample hall and passage echo to the step of busy waiter and guest come and depart the exchange hotel be one of the finest building for its purpose in the united state and be extremely well-kept i pay a visit to the capitol nobly situate on a eminence which overlook the city and be plant with tree the statue of washington execute by houdon for the state of virginium in 1788 be here it be of the size of life represent gen washington in the costume of he day and in a ordinary standing posture it gratify curiosity but raise no particular moral emotion compare with the statue by greenough it present a good example of the difference between the work of a mere sculptor -- skillful indeed but still a mere sculptor -- and the work of a man of genius i shall shortly set out for charleston south carolina letter x a journey from richmond to charleston charleston _ march _ 6 1843 i leave richmond on the afternoon of a keen march day in the railway train for petersburg where we arrive after dark and therefore could form no judgment of the appearance of the town here we be transfer to another train of car among the passenger be a lecturer on mesmerism with he wife and a young woman who accompany they as a mesmeric subject the young woman accustom to be easily put to sleep seem to get through the night very comfortably but the spouse of the operator appear to be much disturb by the frequent and capricious opening of the door by the other passenger which let in torrent of intensely cold air from without and chide the offender with a wholesome sharpness about two o'clock in the morning we reach blakely on the roanoke where we be make to get out of the car and be march in long procession for about a quarter of a mile down to the river a negro walk before we to light we way bear a blaze pine torch which scatter spark like a steam-engine and a crowd of negro follow we bear we baggage we go down a steep path to the roanoke where river and be land at a place call weldon here we take the car for wilmington in north carolina and shabby vehicle they be denote we arrival in a milder climate by be extremely uncomfortable for cold weather as morning dawn we see ourselves in the midst of the pine forest of north carolina vast tract of level sand overgrown with the long-leaved pine a tall stately tree with sparse and thick twig end in long brush of leaf murmur in the strong cold wind extend everywhere around we at great distance from each other we pass log-house and sometimes a dwelling of more pretension with a piazza and here and there field in which cotton or maize have be plant last year or a orchard with a few small mossy tree the pool beside the road be cover with ice just form and the negro who like a good fire at almost any season of the year and who find a abundant supply of the finest fuel in these forest have make blaze fire of the resinous wood of the pine wherever they be at work the tract of sandy soil we perceive be intersperse with marsh crowd with cypress-tree and verdant at they border with a growth of evergreen such as the swamp-bay the gallberry the holly and various kind of evergreen creeper which be unknown to we northern climate and which become more frequent as we proceed we pass through extensive forest of pine which have be _ box _ as it be call for the collection of turpentine every tree have be score by the axe upon one of its side some of they as high as the arm could reach down to the root and the broad wound be cover with the turpentine which seem to saturate every fiber of the long-leaved pine sometimes we see large flake or crust of the turpentine of a light-yellow color which have fall and lay beside the tree on the ground the collection of turpentine be a work of destruction it strip acre after acre of these noble tree and if it go on the time be not far distant when the long-leaved pine will become nearly extinct in this region which be so sterile as hardly to be fit for produce any thing else we see large tract cover with the standing trunk of tree already kill by it and other tract beside they have be freshly attack by the spoiler i be tell that the tree which grow up when the long-leaved pine be destroy be the loblolly pine or as it be sometimes call the short-leaved pine a tree of very inferior quality and in little esteem about half-past two in the afternoon we come to wilmington a little town build upon the white sand of cape fear some of the house stand where not a blade of grass or other plant can grow a few evergreen oak in place pleasantly overhang the water here we take the steamer for charleston i may as well mention here a fraud which be sometimes practice upon those who go by this route to charleston advertisement be distribute at new york and elsewhere inform the public that the fare from baltimore to charleston by the railway through washington and richmond be but twenty-two dollar i take the railway pay from place to place as i go and find that this be a falsehood i be make to pay seven or eight dollar more in the course of my journey i be tell that to protect myself from this imposition i should have purchase at baltimore a through ticket as it be call that be should have pay in advance for the whole distance but the advertisement do not inform I that this be necessary no wonder that trick upon traveller should have become a proverbial expression for they be a much-enduring race more or less plunder in every part of the world the next morning at eight o'clock we find ourselves enter charleston harbor sullivan 's island with fort moultrie breathing recollection of the revolution on we right jame island on we leave in front the stately dwelling of the town and all around on the land side the horizon bound by a apparent belt of evergreen -- the live-oak the water-oak the palmetto the pine and plant about the dwelling the magnolia and the wild orange -- give to the scene a summer aspect the city of charleston strike the visitor from the north most agreeably he perceive at once that he be in a different climate the spacious house be surround with broad piazza often a piazza to each story for the sake of shade and coolness and each house generally stand by itself in a garden plant with tree and shrub many of which preserve they verdure through the winter we see early flower already open the peach and plum-tree be in full bloom and the wild orange as they call the cherry-laurel be just put forth its blossom the building -- some with stuccoed wall some build of large dark-red brick and some of wood -- be not keep fresh with paint like ours but be allow to become weather-stained by the humid climate like those of the european town the street be broad and quiet unpaved in some part but in none as with we offensive both to sight and smell the public building be numerous for the size of the city and well-built in general with sufficient space about they to give they a noble aspect and all the advantage which they could derive from they architecture the inhabitant judge from what i have see of they which be not much i confess do not appear undeserving of the character which have be give they of possess the most polished and agreeable manners of all the american city i may shortly write you again from the interior of south carolina letter xi the interior of south carolina a corn-shucking barnwell district south carolina _ march 29 1843 _ since i last write i have pass three week in the interior of south carolina visit columbium the capital of the state a pretty town roam over a considerable part of barnwell district with some part of the neighbor one of orangeburg enjoy the hospitality of the planter -- very agreeable and intelligent man be out in a racoon hunt be present at a corn-shucking listen to negro ballad negro joke and the banjo witness negro dance see two alligator at least and eat bushel of hominy whoever come out on the railroad to this district a distance of seventy mile or more if he be to judge only by what he see in he passage might naturally take south carolina for a vast pine-forest with here and there a clearing make by some enterprising settler and would wonder where the cotton which clothes so many million of the human race be produce the railway keep on a tract of sterile sand overgrown with pine pass here and there along the edge of a morass or cross a stream of yellow water a lonely log-house under these old tree be a sight for sore eye and only two or three plantation properly so call meet the eye in the whole distance the cultivate and more productive land lie apart from this tract near stream and intersperse with more frequent pond and marsh here you find plantation comprise several thousand of acre a considerable part of which always lie in forest cotton and corn field of vast extent and a negro village on every plantation at a respectful distance from the habitation of the proprietor evergreen tree of the oak family and other which i mention in my last letter be generally plant about the mansion some of they be surround with dreary clearing full of the standing trunk of dead pine other be pleasantly situate in the edge of wood intersect by wind path a ramble or a ride -- a ride on a hand-gallop it should be -- in these pine wood on a fine march day when the weather have all the spirit of we march day without its severity be one of the most delightful recreation in the world the path be upon a white sand which when not frequently travel be very firm under foot on all side you be surround by noble stem of tree tower to a immense height from whose summit far above you the wind be draw deep and grand harmony and often you way be beside a marsh verdant with magnolia where the yellow jessamine now in flower fill the air with fragrance and the bamboo-briar a evergreen creeper twine itself with various other plant which never shed they leaf in winter these wood abound in game which you will believe I when i say i have rather start than shoot -- flock of turtle-dove rabbit rise and scud before you bevy of quail partridge they call they here chirp almost under you horse 's foot wild duck swim in the pool and wild turkey which be frequently shoot by the practice sportsman but you must hear of the corn-shucking the one at which i be present be give on purpose that i might witness the humor of the carolina negro a huge fire of _ light-wood _ be make near the corn-house light-wood be the wood of the long-leaved pine and be so call not because it be light for it be almost the heaviest wood in the world but because it give more light than any other fuel in clear land the pine be girdle and suffer to stand the outer portion of the wood decay and fall off the inner part which be saturate with turpentine remain upright for year and constitute the planter 's provision of fuel when a supply be want one of these dead trunk be fell by the axe the abundance of light-wood be one of the boast of south carolina wherever you be if you happen to be chilly you may have a fire extempore a bit of light-wood and a coal give you a bright blaze and a strong heat in a instant the negro make fire of it in the field where they work and when the morning be wet and chilly in the pen where they be milk the cow at a plantation where i pass a frosty night i see fire in a small inclosure and be tell by the lady of the house that she have order they to be make to warm the cattle the light-wood fire be make and the negro drop in from the neighbor plantation singing as they come the driver of the plantation a color man bring out basket of corn in the husk and pile it in a heap and the negro begin to strip the husk from the ear singing with great glee as they work keep time to the music and now and then throw in a joke and a extravagant burst of laughter the song be generally of a comic character but one of they be set to a singularly wild and plaintive air which some of we musician would do well to reduce to notation these be the word : johnny come down de hollow oh hollow johnny come down de hollow oh hollow de nigger-trader get I oh hollow de speculator buy I oh hollow i be sell for silver dollar oh hollow boy go catch de pony oh hollow bring he round de corner oh hollow i be goin ' away to georgia oh hollow boy good-by forever oh hollow the song of jenny go away be also give and another call the monkey-song probably of african origin in which the principal singer personate a monkey with all sort of odd gesticulation and the other negro bear part in the chorus dan dan who be de dandy one of the song commonly sing on these occasion represent the various animal of the wood as belong to some profession or trade for example -- de cooter be de boatman -- the cooter be the terrapin and a very expert boatman he be de cooter be de boatman john john crow de red-bird de soger john john crow de mocking-bird de lawyer john john crow de alligator sawyer john john crow the alligator 's back be furnish with a toothed ridge like the edge of a saw which explain the last line when the work of the evening be over the negro adjourn to a spacious kitchen one of they take he place as musician whistle and beat time with two stick upon the floor several of the man come forward and execute various dance caper prance and drum with heel and toe upon the floor with astonishing agility and perseverance though all of they have perform they daily task and have work all the evening and some have walk from four to seven mile to attend the corn-shucking from the dance a transition be make to a mock military parade a sort of burlesque of we militia training in which the word of command and the evolution be extremely ludicrous it become necessary for the commander to make a speech and confess he incapacity for public speaking he call upon a huge black man name toby to address the company in he stead toby a man of powerful frame six foot high he face ornament with a beard of fashionable cut have hitherto stand lean against the wall look upon the frolic with a air of superiority he consent come forward demand a bit of paper to hold in he hand and harangue the soldiery it be evident that toby have listen to stump-speech in he day he speak of de majority of sous carolina de interest of de state de honor of ole ba ` nwell district and these phrase he connect by various expletive and sound of which we could make nothing a length he begin to falter when the captain with admirable presence of mind come to he relief and interrupted and close the harangue with a hurrah from the company toby be allow by all the spectator black and white to have make a excellent speech the black of this region be a cheerful careless dirty race not hard work and in many respects indulgently treat it be of course the desire of the master that he slave shall be laborious on the other hand it be the determination of the slave to lead as easy a life as he can the master have power of punishment on he side the slave on he have invincible inclination and a thousand expedient learn by long practice the result be a compromise in which each party yield something and a good-natured though imperfect and slovenly obedience on one side be purchase by good treatment on the other i have be tell by planter that the slave bring from africa be much more serviceable though more high-spirited and dangerous than the slave bear in this country and early train to he condition i have be impatiently wait the approach of spring since i come to this state but the weather here be still what the inhabitant call winter the season i be tell be more than three week later than usual field of indian corn which be plant in the beginning of march must be replant for the seed have perish in the ground and the cotton planting be defer for fine weather the peach and plum tree have stand in blossom for week and the forest tree which at this time be usually in full foliage be as bare as in December cattle be die in the field for want of pasture i have thus have a sample of the winter climate of south carolina if never more severe or stormy than i have already experience it must be a agreeable one the custom of sit with open door however i find a little difficult to like at first a door in south carolina except perhaps the outer door of a house be not make to shut it be merely a sort of flapper a ornamental appendage to the opening by which you enter a room a kind of moveable screen make to swing to and fro but never to be secure by a latch unless for some purpose of strict privacy a door be the ventilator to the room the window be not raise except in warm weather but the door be keep open at all season on cold day you have a bright fire of pine-wood blaze before you and a draught of cold air at you back the reason give for this practice be that fresh air be wholesome and that close room occasion cold and consumption letter xii savannah picolata east florida _ April 7 1843 _ as i land at this place a few hour since i step into the midst of summer yesterday morning when i leave its agreeable climate during the sixteen day of my residence here the weather have certainly be as delightful as i could imagine we have the temperature of early June as June be know in new york the climate of florida be in fact a insular climate the atlantic on the east and the gulf of mexico on the west temper the air that blow over it make they cooler in summer and warmer in winter i do not wonder therefore that it be so much the resort of invalid it would be more so if the softness of its atmosphere and the beauty and serenity of its season be generally know nor should it be suppose e _ omo de compliment letter xv a voyage from st augustine to savannah savannah _ April _ 28 1843 on the morning of the 24th we take leave of we good friend in st augustine and embark in the steamer for savannah never be softer or more genial air breathe out of the heaven than those which play around we as we plough the water of the matanza sound pass under the dark wall of the old fort and leave it behind we stand for the passage to the main ocean it be a common say in st augustine that florida be the best poor man 's country in the world and truly i believe that those who live on the shore of this sound find it so its green water teem with life and produce abundance of the finest fish ------ of shell or fin and exquisitest name clam be dig up on the pure sand along the beach where the fisherman drag they boat ashore and wherever the salt water dash there be a oyster if he can find aught upon which to anchor he habitation along the edge of the marsh next to the water you see a row -- a wall i should rather say -- of oyster apparently sprout one out of another as high as the tide flow they be call here though i do not know why ratoon oyster the abundance of fish solve the problem which have puzzle many how the minorcan population of st augustine live now that they orange-tree upon which they formerly depend be unproductive in the steamboat be two or three person who have visit florida with a view of purchase land now that the indian war be end colonization have revive and people be throng into the country to take advantage of the law which assign a hundred and sixty acre to every actual settler in another year the influx of population will probably be still greater though the confusion and uncertainty which exist in regard to the title of the land will somewhat obstruct the settlement of the country before the spanish government cede it to the united state they make numerous grant to individual intend to cover all the best land of the territory many of the land grant have never be survey and they situation and limit be very uncertain the settler therefore if he be not very careful may find he farm overlaid by a old spanish claim i have say that the war be end although the seminole chief sam jone and about seventy of he people remain the country be in profound peace from one end to the other and you may traverse the part most distant from the white settlement without the least danger or molestation from the indian how be it i ask one day of a gentleman who have long reside in st augustine that after what have happen you can think it safe new country he would certainly be put to death it be he interest therefore to propitiate the favor of the whites by the most unexceptionable behavior for he life depend upon be allow to remain there be yet another reason which you will understand from what i be about to say before the war break out the indian of this country those very man who suddenly become so bloodthirsty and so formidable be a quiet and inoffensive race badly treat for the most part by the whites and passively submit to ill treatment without any appearance of feeling or spirit when they at length resolve upon war they conceal they family in the island of the everglade whither they suppose the whites would never be able to follow they they rule of warfare be this never to endanger the life of one of they warrior for the sake of gain the greatest advantage over they enemy they strike only when they feel themselves in perfect safety if they see a opportunity of destroy twenty white man by the sacrifice of a single indian the whites be allow to escape act on this principle if they retreat have be as inaccessible as they suppose it they would have keep up the warfare until they have drive the whites out of the territory when however general worth introduce a new method of prosecute the war follow up the indian with a close and perpetual pursuit chase they into they great shallow lake the everglade and to its most secret island they see at once that they be conquer they see that further hostility be hopeless and return to they former submissive and quiet demeanor it be well perhaps add my friend in a kind of postscript that a few indian should remain in florida they be the best hunter of runaway slave in the world and may save we from a maroon war the indian name of the everglade i be tell signify grass-water a term which well express its appearance it be a vast lake broader by thousand of acre in a wet than in a dry season and so shallow that the grass everywhere grow from the bottom and overtop its surface the bottom be of hard sand so firm that it can be ford almost everywhere on horseback and here and there be deep channel which the traveller cross by swim he horse general worth 's success in quell the insurrection of the seminole have make he very popular in florida where the energy and sagacity with which the closing campaign of the war be conduct be speak of in the highest
