the lunatic asylum at hanwell london _ July _ 28 1845 since we come to england we have visit the lunatic asylum at hanwell in the neighborhood of london it be a large building divide into numerous apartment with the plainest accommodation for the insane poor of the county of middlesex it be superintend by dr conolly who be most admirably fit for the place he fill by he great humanity sagacity and ingenuity i put these quality together as necessary to each other mere humanity without tact and skill would fail deplorably the rude and coarse method of government which consist in severity be the most obvious one they suggest themselves to the dullest mind and cost nothing but bodily strength to put they in execution the gentler method require reflection knowledge and dexterity it be these which dr conolly apply with perfect success he have take great pain to make himself acquaint by personal observation with the treatment of the insane in different hospital not only in england but on the continent he find that to be the most efficacious which interfere least with they personal liberty and on this principle the truth of which a experience of several year have now confirm he found the system of treatment at hanwell we have letter to dr conolly with the kindness and gentleness of whose manners we be much strike he conduct we over the several ward of the asylum we find in it a thousand person of both sex not one of whom be in seclusion that be to say confine because it be dangerous to allow he to go at large nor be they subject to any apparent restraint whatever some be engage in read some in exercise and game of skill of the female some be occupy in sew other at work in the kitchen or the laundry melancholic patient be walk about in silence or sit gloomily by themselves idiot be rock they body backward and forward as they sit but all be peaceable in they demeanor and the greatest quiet prevail no chastisement of any kind be inflict the lunatic be always treat as a patient and never as a offender when he become so outrageous and violent that he presence can be endure no longer he be put into a room with padded wall and floor where he can do himself no mischief and where he rage be allow to exhale even the straight jacket be unknown here i say that the demeanor of all the patient with whom the asylum be swarm be peaceable there be one exception on enter one of the ward a girl of a earnest and determine aspect as soon as she see dr conolly begin to scream violently and spring towards he thrust aside the bystander by main force two of the female attendant come immediately up and strive to appease she hold she back without severity as a mother would restrain she infant i see they struggle with she for some time how they finally dispose of she i do not observe but she scream have cease before we leave the ward among the patient be one who we be tell be remarkable for he extravagant love of finery and whose cell be plaster over with glare color print and patch of color paper ornamentally dispose he wear on he hat a broad strip of tarnished lace and have decorate he waistcoat with several perpendicular row of pearl button you have make you room very fine here say the doctor yes say he smile and evidently delighted but my dear sir all be vanity -- all be vanity sir and vexation of spirit there be but one thing that we ought to strive for and that be the kingdom of heaven as there be no disputing this proposition we pass on to another cell at the door of which stand a tall erect personage who be busy with a pot of paint and a brush inscribe the pannel with motto and scrap of verse the wall of he room be cover with poetry and pithy sentence some of the latter appear to be of he own composition and be not badly turn they purport generally be this : that birth be but a trivial accident and that virtue and talent be the only true nobility this man be find wander about in chiswick full of a plan for educate the prince of wale in a manner to enable he to fill the throne with credit and usefulness as he name could not be learn the appellation of chiswick be give he which he have himself adopt style himself mr chiswick in he motto but always take care to put the name between inverted comma as we proceed a man rise from he seat and lay both hand on a table before he so as to display he finger ornament with ring make of black ribbon in which glass button be set for jewel address dr conolly with great respect formally set forth that he be in great want of a new coat for sunday the one he have on be positively unfit to appear in and that a better have be promise he the doctor stop inquire into the case and the poor fellow be gratify by the assurance that the promise coat should be speedily forthcoming in he progress through the ward dr conolly listen with great patience to the various complaint of the inmate one of they come up and tell we that he do not think the method of the institution judicious the patient say he be many of they grow worse one in particular who have be here for several week i can see be grow worse every day dr conolly ask the name of this patient -- i can not tell say the man but i can bring he to you bring he then say the doctor and after a moment 's absence he return lead up one of the healthiest and quietest look man in the ward he look better to be sure say the man but he be the female ward we be introduce as gentleman from america to a respectable-looking old lady in black who sit with a crutch by she side be you not lawyer she ask and when we assure she that we be only yankee she rebuke we mildly for assume such a disguise when she know very well that we be a couple of attorney and you doctor she add i be surprise that you should have any thing to do with such a dupe of these people it be in the same ward i think that a well-dressed woman in a bonnet and shawl be promenade the room carry a bible and two smaller volume apparently prayer or hymn book have you i be let out of this place say a voice as we enter one of the ward dr conolly tell I that he have several irish patient in the asylum and that they give he the most trouble on account of the hurry in which they be to be discharge we hear the same request eagerly make in the same brogue by various other patient of both sex as i leave this multitude of lunatic promiscuously gather from the poor and the reduce class comprise all variety of mental disease from idiocy to madness yet all of they hold in such admirable order by the law of kindness that to the casual observer most of they betray no symptom of insanity and of the rest many appear to be only very odd people quietly pursue they own harmless whim i could not but feel the highest veneration for the enlighten humanity by which the establishment be direct i consider also if the feeling of personal liberty the absence of physical restraint and the power of moral motive have such power to hold together in perfect peace and order even a promiscuous band of lunatic how much greater must be they influence over the mind of man in a state of sanity and on how false a foundation rest all the government of force the true basis of human polity appoint by god in we nature be the power of moral motive which be but another term for public opinion of the political controversy which at present agitate the country the corn-law question be that which call forth the most feel i mean on the part of those who oppose the restriction on the introduction of foreign grain -- for on the other side it appear to I that the battle be languidly fight nothing can exceed the enthusiasm of the adversary of the corn-law with some of they the repeal of the tax on bread be the remedy for all political evil free trade free trade be the burden of they conversation and although a friend of free trade myself to the last and uttermost limit i have be in circle in england in which i have a little too much of it yet this be a example to prove what a strong hold the question have take of the mind of man and how completely the thought of many be absorb by it against such a feeling as that which have be kindle in great britain on the corn-law question no law in we country could stand so far as i can judge it be spread as well as grow stronger i be tell that many of the farmer have become proselyte of the league the league be a powerful and prodigiously numerous association with ample and increase fund publish able tract support well-conducted journal and hold crowded public meeting which be address by some of the ablest speaker in the unite kingdom i attend one of these at covent garden stage pit box and gallery of that large building be fill with one of the most respectable-looking audience man and woman i have ever see among the speaker of the evening be cobden and fox cobden in physiognomy and appearance might almost pass for a american and have a certain new england sharpness and shrewdness in he way of deal with a subject he address be argumentative yet there be a certain popular clearness about it a fertility of familiar illustration and a earnest feeling which make it uncommonly impressive fox be one of the most fluent and ingenious speaker i ever hear in a popular assembly both be listen to by a audience which seem to hang on every word that fall from they lip the musical world here be talk about colman 's improvement in the piano i have see the instrument which the inventor bring out from america it be furnish with a row of brass reed like those of the instrument call the seraphine these take up the sound make by the string of the piano and prolong it to any degree which be desire it be a splicing of the sound of one instrument upon another yet if the invention be to be leave where it be in colman 's instrument it could not succeed with the public the note of the reed be too harsh and nasal and want the sweetness and mellowness of tone which belong to the string of the piano at present the invention be in the hand of mr rand the portrait painter a countryman of ours who be one of the most ingenious mechanician in the world he have improve the tone of the reed till they rival in softness and fullness those of the string and in fact can hardly be distinguish from they so that the sound of the two instrument run into one another without any apparent difference mr rand have contrived three or four different machine for make the reed with dispatch and precision and if the difficulty of keep the string which be undergo a constant relaxation in perfect unison with the reed can be overcome i see nothing to prevent the most complete and brilliant success letter xxvii change in paris paris _ August _ 9 1845 my last letter be date at london in my passage across england i king of wirtemberg letter xxx buffalo -- cleveland -- detroit steamer oregon lake huron off thunder bay _ July _ 24 1846 as i approach the city of buffalo the other morning from the east i find myself oblige to confess that much of the beauty of a country be owe to the season for twenty or thirty mile before we reach lake erie the field of this fertile region look more and more arid and sun-scorched and i could not but contrast they appearance with that of the neighborhood of new york where in a district comparatively sterile a uncommonly showery season have keep the herbage fresh and deep and make the tree heavy with leaf here on the contrary i see meadow tinge by the drought with a reddish hue pasture graze to the root of the grass and tree spread what seem to I a meager shade yet the harvest of wheat and even of hay in western new york be say to be by no means scanty buffalo continue to extend on every side but the late addition to the city do not much improve its emigrant along unpaved and powdery street i see however on a little excursion which i make into the surrounding country that pleasant little neighborhood be rise up at no great distance with they neat house they young tree and they new shrubbery they have a fine building material at buffalo -- a sort of brown stone easily work -- but i be sorry to see that most of the house build of it both in the town and country seem to have stand for several year we visit the new fort which the government be erect on the lake a little to the north of the town command the entrance of niagara river it be small but of wonderful apparent strength with wall of prodigious thickness and so sturdy in its defence that it seem to I one might as well think of cannonade the cliff of weehawken it be curious to see how be batter down before breakfast the finest old fort of the last century be now find to be unsafe against attack that which we have at st augustine be a uncommonly good sample of its kind but when i be _ July _ 25 1846 soon after pass the flat describe in my last letter and enter the river st clair the steamer stop to take in wood on the canadian side here i go on shore all that we could see of the country be a road along the bank a row of cottage at a considerable distance from each other along the road a narrow belt of clear field behind they and beyond the field the original forest standing like a long lofty wall with its crowded stem of enormous size and immense height root in the strong soil -- ash and maple and elm the largest of they species scatter in the foreground be number of leafless elm so huge that the settler as if in despair of bring they to the ground by the ax have girdle they and leave they to decay and fall at they leisure we go up to one of the house before which he tell we of a indian settlement about twenty mile further up the st clair here dwell a remnant of the chippewa tribe collect by the canadian government which have build for they comfortable log-house with chimney furnish they with horse and neat cattle and utensil of agriculture erect a house of worship and give they a missionary the design of plant they here saidth esettler be to encourage they to cultivate the soil and what have be the success of the plan i ask it have meet with no success at all he answer the worst thing that the government could do for these people be to give they every thing as it have do and leave they under no necessity to provide for themselves they chop over a little land a acre or two to a family they squaw plant a little corn and a few bean and this be the extent of they agriculture they pass they time in hunting and fishing or in idleness they find deer and bear in the wood behind they and fish in the st clair before they door and they squander they yearly pension in one respect they be just like white man they will not work if they can live without what fish do they find in the st clair various sort trout and white-fish be the finest but they be not so abundant at this season sturgeon and pike be just now in season and the pike be excellent one of we happen to observe that the river might easily be cross by swim the settler answer : not so easily as you might think the river be as cold as a well and the swimmer would soon be chill through and perhaps take with the cramp it be this coldness of the water which make the fish so fine at this season this mention of sturgeon tempt I to relate a anecdote which i hear as i be come up the hudson a gentleman who live east of the river a little back of tivolus catch last spring one of these fish which weigh about a hundred and sixty pound he carry it to a large pond near he house the longest diameter of which be about a mile and without take it out of the net in which he have catch it he knot part of the mesh closely around it and attach they to a pair of line like rein put the creature into the water to the end of the line he have take care to attach a buoy to mark the place of the fish in the pond he keep a small boat and when he have a mind to make a water-excursion he row to the place where the buoy be float tie the line to the boat and pull they so as to disturb the fish be draw backward and forward with great rapidity over the surface the pond in its deepest part have only seven foot water so that there be no danger of be drag under we now proceed up the river and in about two hour come to a neat little village on the british side with a windmill a little church and two or three little cottage prettily screen by young tree immediately beyond this be the beginning of the chippewa settlement of which we have be tell log-house at the distance of nearly a quarter of a mile from each other stand in a long row beside the river with scatter tree about they the largest of the forest some girdle and leafless some untouched and green the smallest tree between have be cut away here and there a indian woman in a blue dress and bare-headed be walk along the road cow and horse be grazing near the house patch of maize be see tend in a slovenly manner and by no means clear of bush but nobody be at work in the field two female come down to the bank with paddle and put off into the river in a birch-bark canoe the end of which be carve in the peculiar indian fashion a little beyond stand a group of boy and girl on the water 's edge the boy in shirt and leggin silently watch the steamer as it shoot by they still further on a group of child of both sex seven in number come run with shrill cry down the bank it be then about twelve o'clock and the weather be extremely sultry the boy in a instant throw off they shirt and leggin and plunge into the water with shout but the girl be in before they for they wear only a kind of petticoat which they do not take off but cast themselves into the river at once and slide through the clear water like seal this little indian colony on the edge of the forest extend for several mile along the river where its bank be highest and best adapt to the purpose of settlement it end at last just below the village which bear the name of fort sarana in the neighborhood of which i be show a odd-looking and other evergreen it be contrary to the law to kill they after the christmas holiday but sometimes they retreat be invade and a deer or two kill they flesh however be not wholesome on account of the laurel leave on which they feed and they skin be nearly worthless i express my surprise that the leaf of the mountain laurel the _ kalmium latifolium _ which be so deadly to sheep should be the winter food of the deer it be because the deer have no gall answer the man that the pison do not take effect but they meat will not do to eat except in a small quantity and cook with pork which i think help take the pison out of it the deer he go on to say be now pass out of the blue into the gray after the holiday when they hair become long and they winter coat be quite grow they hide be soft and tender and tear easily when dress and it would be folly to kill they even if there be no law against it he go on to find a parallel to the case of the deer-skin in the hide of neat-cattle which when bring from a hot country like south america be firmer and tougher than when obtain in a colder climate like ours the wyoming traveller give a bad account of the health just at present of the beautiful which hitherto have be almost uniformly healthy here however in stroudsburg and its neighborhood they boast that the fever and ague have never yet make its appearance i be glad to hear a good account of the pecuniary circumstance of the pennsylvanium farmer they get in debt like every body else during the prosperous year of 1835 and 1836 and have be ever since work themselves gradually out of it i have never say a intelligent gentleman of stroudsburg know the owner of the farm so free from debt and so generally easy and prosperous in they condition as at this moment it be to be hope that have we driver stop a moment at a inn to give water to he horse the bar-room be full of fresh-colored young man in military uniform talk pennsylvanium german rather rapidly and vociferously they surround a floor with a fiddle and begin to play about a dozen of the young man stand up on the floor in couple face each other and hammer out the tune with they foot give a tread or tap on the floor to into we vehicle and in somewhat more than a hour be enter the little village of nazareth pleasantly situate among field the autumnal verdure of which indicate they fertility nazareth be a moravian village of four or five hundred inhabitant look prodigiously like a little town of the old world except that it be more neatly keep the house be square and solid of stone or brick build immediately on row of iron mill with the canal on one side and the lehigh on the other one of these be a roll mill into which we could look from the bank where we stand and observe the whole process of the manufacture which be very striking the whole interior of the building be light at night only by the mouth of several furnace which be kindle to a white heat out of one of these a thick bar of iron about six foot in length and heat to a perfect whiteness be draw and one end of it present to the cylinder of the mill which seize it and draw it through between they roll out to three or four times its original size a sooty workman grasp the opposite end of the bar with pincers as soon as it be fairly through and return it again to the cylinder which deliver it again on the opposite side in this way it pass backward and forward till it be roll into a enormous length and shoot across the black floor with a twining motion like a serpent of fire at last when press to the proper thinness and length it be coil up into a circle by the help of a machine contrived for the purpose which roll it up as a shopkeeper roll up a ribbon we find a man near where we stand begrime by the soot of the furnace handle the clumsy mass of iron which bear the name of bloom the roll mill he say belong to rodenbough stewart & co who have very extensive contract for furnish iron to the nailmaker and wire manufacturer will they stop the mill for the new tariff say i they will stop for nothing reply the man the new tariff be a good tariff if people would but think so it cost the iron-masters fifteen dollar a ton to make they iron and they sell it for forty dollar a ton if the new tariff oblige they to sell it for considerable less they will still make money so revolve the cycle of opinion twenty year ago a pennsylvanian who question the policy of the protective system would have be look upon as a sort of curiosity now the bloomers and stable-boy begin to talk free trade what will they talk passage by sea from new york to savannah would seem to afford little matter for a letter yet those who take the trouble to read what i be about to write will i hope admit that there be some thing to be observe even on such a voyage it be indeed a remarkably quiet one and worthy of note on that account if on no other we have a quiet vessel quiet weather a quiet good-natured captain a quiet crew and remarkably quiet passenger when we leave the wharf at new york last week in the good steamship tennessee we be not conscious at first as we sit in the cabin that she be in motion and proceed down the harbor there be no beating or churning of the sea no struggle to get forward she paddle play in the water as smoothly as those of a terrapin without jar or noise the tennessee be one of the tightest and strongest boat that navigate we coast the very flooring of she deck be compose of timber instead of plank and help to keep she massive frame more compactly and solidly together it be she first voyage she fifty-one passenger loll on sofa fresh from the upholsterer 's and sleep on mattress which have never be press by the human form before in state-room where foul air have never collect nor be it possible that the air should become impure in they to any great degree for the tennessee be the best-ventilated ship i ever be in the main cabin and the state-room be connect with each other and with the deck by numerous opening and pipe which keep up a constant circulation of air in every part i have speak of the passenger as remarkably quiet person several of they i believe never speak two of the most remarkable two spinster sarah and phebe comstock relative of mine and friend of my youth of whom i retain a vivid recollection they be in opulent circumstance for the neighborhood in which they live possess a farm of about two hundred acre they be industrious frugal and extremely charitable but they never relieve a poor family without visit it and inquire carefully into its circumstance sarah be the housekeeper and phebe the farmer phebe know nothing of kitchen matter but she know at what time of the year greensward should be break up and corn plant and potato dig she drop indian corn and sow english grain with she own hand in the time of planting or of harvest it be sarah who visit and relieve the poor i remember that they have various way of employ the young people who call upon they if it be late in the autumn there be a chopping-board and chopping-knife ready with the foot of neat-cattle from which the oily part have be extract by boil ` you do not want to be idle ' they would say ` chop this meat and you shall have you share of the mince-pie that we be go to make ' at other time a supply of old woollen stocking be ready for unraveling ` we know you do not care to be idle ' they would say ` here be some stocking which you would oblige we by unravel ' if you ask what use they make of the spool of woollen thread obtain by this process they would answer : ` we use it as the weft of the linsey-woolsey with which we clothe we negro ' they have negro slave in those time and old tone a faithful black servant of theirs who have see more than a hundred year be alive yet they practice one very peculiar piece of economy the white hickory you know yield the purest and sweetest of saccharine juice they have they hickory fuel cut into short billet which before place on the fire they lay on the andiron a little in front of the blaze so as to subject it to a pretty strong heat this cause the syrup in the wood to drop from each end of the billet where it be catch in a cup and in this way a gallon or two be collect in the course of a fortnight with this they flavor they finest cake they die about thirty year since one at the age of eighty-nine and the other at the age of ninety on the tomb-stone of one of they it be record that she have be a member of the church for seventy year they father be a remarkable man in he way he be a rich man in he time and keep a park of deer one of the last know in connecticut for the purpose of supply he table with venison he pride himself on the strict and literal fulfillment of he word on one occasion he have a law-suit with one of he neighbor before a justice of the peace in which he be cast and order to pay ten shilling damages and a shilling as the fee of court he pay the ten shilling and ask the justice whether he would allow he to pay the remain shilling when he next pass he door the magistrate readily consent but from that time old comstock never go by he house whenever he have occasion to go to church or to any other place the direct road to which lead by the justice 's door he be careful to take a lane which pass behind the dwelling and at some distance from it the have time before depart from cuba and i find that i have a few moment to spare for a hasty letter the people of augusta boast of the beauty of they place and not without some reason the street be broad and in some part overshadow with row of fine tree the bank of the river on which it stand be high and firm and slope half cover with forest of a pleasant aspect overlook it from the west and from the carolina side to the south stretch a broad champaign country on which be some of the finest plantation of georgium i visit one of these consist of ten thousand acre keep throughout in as perfect order as a small farm at the north though large enough for a german principality but what interested I most be a visit to a cotton mill in the neighborhood -- a sample of a class of manufacture establishment where the poor white people of this state and of south carolina find occupation it be a large manufactory and the machinery be in as perfect order as in any of the mill at the north here say a gentleman who accompany we as we enter the long apartment in the second story you will see a sample of the brunette of the piny wood the girl of various age who be employ at the spindle have for the most part a sallow sickly complexion and in many of they face i remark that look of mingled distrust and dejection which often accompany the condition of extreme hopeless poverty these poor girl say one of we party think themselves extremely fortunate to be employ here and accept work gladly they come from the most barren part of carolina and georgium where they family live wretchedly often upon unwholesome food and as idly as wretchedly for hitherto there have be no manual occupation provide for they from which they do not shrink as disgraceful on account of its be the occupation of slave in these factory negro be not employ as operative and this give the calling of the factory girl a certain dignity you would be surprise to see the change which a short time effect in these poor people they come barefooted dirty and in rag they be scour put into shoe and stocking set at work and send regularly to the sunday-school where they be teach what none of they have be teach before -- to read and write in a short time they become expert at they work they lose they sullen shyness and they physiognomy become comparatively open and cheerful they family be relieve from the temptation to theft and other shameful course which accompany the condition of poverty without occupation they have a good deal of the poke-easy manner of the piny wood about they yet say one of we party a georgian it be true i perceive that they have not yet acquire all that alacrity and quickness in they work which you see in the work-people of the new england mill in one of the upper story i see a girl of a clearer complexion than the rest with two long curl swing behind each ear as she step about with the air of a duchess that girl be from the north say we conductor at first we place a expert operative from the north in each story of the building as a instructor and pattern to the rest i have since learn that some attempt be make at first to induce the poor white people to work side by side with the black in these mill these utterly fail and the question then become with the proprietor whether they should employ black only or whites only whether they should give these poor people a occupation which while it tend to elevate they condition secure a more expert class of work-people than the negro could be expect to become or whether they should rely upon the less intelligent and more negligent service of slave they decide at length upon banish the labor of black from they mill at graniteville in south carolina about ten mile from the savannah river a neat little manufacturing village have lately be build up where the family of the _ cracker _ as they be call reclaim from they idle life in the wood be settle and white labor only be employ the enterprise be say to be in a most prosperous condition only coarse cloth be make in these mill -- strong thick fabric suitable for negro shirting -- and the demand for this kind of goods i be tell be greater than the supply every yard make in this manufactory at augusta be take off as soon as it leave the loom i fall in with a northern man in the course of the day who tell I that these mill have drive the northern manufacturer of coarse cotton out of the southern market the building be erect here more cheaply he continue there be far less expense in fuel and the wages of the workpeople be less at first the boy and girl of the cracker family be engage for little more than they board they wages be now better but they be still low i be about to go to the north and i shall do my best to persuade some of my friend who have be almost ruin by this southern competition to come to augusta and set up cotton mill there be water-power at augusta sufficient to turn the machinery of many large establishment a canal from the savannah river bring in a large volume of water which pass from level to level and might be make to turn the spindle and drive the loom of a populous manufacturing town such it will become if any faith be to be place in present indication and a considerable manufacturing population will be settle at this place draw from the half-wild inhabitant of the most barren part of the southern state i look upon the introduction of manufacture at the south as a event of the most favorable promise for that part of the country since it both condense a class of population too thinly scatter to have the benefit of the institution of civilized life of education and religion -- and restore one branch of labor at least to its proper dignity in a region where manual labor have be the badge of servitude and dependence one of the pleasantest spot in the neighborhood of augusta be somerville a sandy eminence cover with wood the shade of which be carefully cherish and in the midst of which be numerous cottage and country seat closely embower in tree with pleasant path lead to they from the highway here the evening in summer be not so oppressively hot as in the town below and dense as the shade be the air be dry and elastic hither many family retire during the hot season and many reside here the year round we drive through it as the sun be set and call at the dwelling of several of the hospitable inhabitant the next morning the railway train bring we to barnwell district in south carolina where i write this i intend to send you some note of the agricultural change which i have observe in this part of south carolina since i be last here but i have hardly time to do it the culture of wheat have be introduce many planter now raise enough for they own consumption the sugar cane be also plant and quantity of sugar and molasses be often make sufficient to supply the plantation on which it be cultivate spinning-wheel and loom have come into use and a strong and durable cotton cloth be weave by the negro woman for the wear of the slave all this show a desire to make the most of the recource of the country and to protect the planter against the embarrassment which often arise from the fluctuate price of the great staple of the south -- cotton but i have no time to dwell upon this subject to-morrow i sail for cuba letter xlv the florida coast -- key west havana _ April _ 7 1849 it be a most agreeable voyage which i make in the steamer isabel to this port the wind in we favor the whole distance fine bright weather the temperature pass gradually from what we have it in new york at the end of may to what it be in the middle of June the isabel be a noble sea-boat of great strength not so well ventilate as the tennessee in which we come to savannah with the second which be also the second of the month we be run rapidly down the florida coast with the trade-wind fresh on we beam sweep before it a long swell from the east in which we vessel rock too much for the stomach of most of the passenger the next day the sea be smoother we have change we direction somewhat and be go before the wind the florida reef full in sight with they long streak of white surf beyond which along the line of the shore lay a belt of water of bright translucent green and in front the wave wear a amethystine tint we sit the greater part of the day under a awning a long line with a baited hook at the end be let down into the water from the stern of we vessel and after be drag there a hour or two it be seize by a king-fish which be immediately haul on board it be a elegantly shaped fish weigh nearly twenty pound with a long head and scale shine with blue and purple it be serve up for dinner and its flavor much commend by the amateur the water around we be full of sail gleam in the sunshine they belong say we charleston pilot to the wrecker who live at key west every morning they come out and cruise among the reef to discover if there be any vessel wreck or of relieve vessel in distress navigate this dangerous coast and bring in such as be wreck the population of course increase with the commerce of the country and every vessel that sail from we port to the gulf of mexico or come from the gulf to the north every addition to the intercourse of the atlantic port with mobile new orlean the west indy or central america add to they chance of gain these people neither plant nor sow they isle be a low barren spot surround by a beach of white sand form of disintegrated porous limestone and a covering of the same sand spread thinly over the rock form its soil it be a scandal say the pilot that this coast be not better light a few light-house would make its navigation much safer and they would be build if florida have any man in congress to represent the matter properly to the government i have long be familiar with this coast -- sixty time at least i have make the voyage from charleston to havana and i be sure that there be no such dangerous navigation on the coast of the united state in go to havana or to new orlean or to other port on the gulf commander of vessel try to avoid the current of the gulf-stream which would carry they to the north and they therefore shave the florida coast and keep near the reef which you see yonder they often strike the reef inadvertently or be drive against they by storm in return northward the navigation be safer we give a good offing to the reef and strike out into the gulf-stream the current of which carry we in the direction of we voyage a little before nine o'clock we have enter the little harbor of key west and be moor in its still water it be a bright moonlight evening and we ramble two or three hour about the town and the island the hull of a dismasted vessel lay close by we inhabitant have four place of worship -- a episcopal a catholic a methodist and a baptist church and the drinking-house which we see open with such a elaborate display of bottle and decanter be not resort to by the people of the place but be the haunt of english and american sailor whom the disaster or the regular voyage of they vessel have bring hither he give we a account of the hurricane of September 1846 which overflow and lay waste the island here where we stand say he the water be four foot deep at least i save my family in a boat and carry they to a higher part of the island two none since a bell be ring about this time and we ask the reason it be to signify that the negro must be at they home answer the man we inquire if there be many black in the place till lately he reply they to key west to be employ by the government we do not want they here and wish that the government would leave they on the hand of they master on the fourth morning when we go on deck the coast of baggage through the custom-house letter xlvi havana havana _ April _ 10 1849 i find that it require a greater effort of resolution to sit down to the writing of a long letter in this soft climate than in the country i have leave i feel a temptation to sit idly and let the grateful wind from the sea come in at the broad window flow around I or read or talk as i happen to have a book or a companion that there be something in a tropical climate which indispose one to vigorous exertion i can well believe from what i experience in myself and what i see around I the lady do not seem to take the least exercise except a occasional drive on the paseo or public park they never walk out and when they be shop which be no less the vocation of they sex here than in other civilized country they never descend from they _ volante _ but the goods be bring out by the obsequious shopkeeper and the lady make she choice and discuss the price as she sit in she carriage yet the woman of cuba show no token of delicate health freshness of color do not belong to a latitude so near the equator but they have plump figure placid unwrinkled countenance a well-developed bust and eye the brilliant languor of which be not the languor of illness the girl as well as the young man have rather narrow shoulder but as they advance in life the chest in the woman particularly seem to expand from year to year till it attain a amplitude by no means common in we country i fully believe that this effect and they general health in spite of the inaction in which they pass they life be owe to the free circulation of air through they apartment for in cuba the woman as well as the man may be say to live in the open air they know nothing of close room in all the island and nothing of foul air and to this i have no doubt quite as much as to the mildness of the temperature the friendly effect of its climate upon invalid from the north be to be ascribe they ceiling be extremely lofty and the wide window extend from the top of the room to the floor and guard by long perpendicular bar of iron be without glass and when close be generally only close with blind which while they break the force of the wind when it be too strong do not exclude the air since i have be on the island i may be say to have breakfast and dine and sup and sleep in the open air in a atmosphere which be never in repose except for a short time in the morning after sunrise at other time a breeze be always stir in the day-time bringing in the air from the ocean and at night draw it out again to the sea in walk through the street of the town in cuba i have be entertain by the glimpse i have through the ample window of what be go on in the parlor sometimes a curtain hanging before they allow I only a sight of the small hand which about the window of a brilliantly light room and chatter the soft spanish of the island in voice that sound strangely near to I i have speak of they languid posture : they love to recline on sofa they house be fill with rocking-chair import from the united state they be fond of sit in chair tilted against the wall as we sometimes do at home indeed they go beyond we in this respect for in cuba they have invent a kind of chair which by lower the back and raise the knee place the sitter precisely in the posture he would take if he sit in a chair lean backward against a wall it be a luxurious attitude i must own and i do not wonder that it be a favorite with lazy people for it relieve one of all the trouble of keep the body upright it be the woman who form the large majority of the half of whom seem vociferate with all they might i mount one of the outer bench and see one of the bird lay dead by the other in a few minute then be hear the chink of gold and silver piece as the fully revive the government conniving at it make a profit on the slave import from africa and screening from the pursuit of the english the pirate who bring they there could scarcely be any arrangement of coast more favorable for smuggling slave into a country than the island and long peninsula and many channel of the southern shore of cuba here the mangrove thicket send down root into the brine from they long branch that stretch over the water form dense screen on each side of the passage from the main ocean to the inland and render it easy for the slaver and he boat to lurk undiscovered by the english man-of-war during the comparative cessation of the slave-trade a few year since the negro i have be tell be much better treat than before they rise in value and when they die it be find not easy to supply they place they be therefore make much of and every thing be do which it be think would tend to preserve they health and maintain they in bodily vigor if the slave-trade should make they cheap again they life of course will be of less consequence to they owner and they will be subject again to be overtask as it have be say they be before there be certainly great temptation to wear they out in the sugar mill which be keep in motion day and night during half the year namely through the dry season if this be not the healthiest employment in the world say a overseer to I on one of the sugar estate it would kill we all who be engage in it both black and white perhaps you may not know that more than half of the island of cuba have never be reduce to tillage immense tract of the rich black or red mould of the island accumulate on the coral rock be yet wait the hand of the planter to be convert into profitable sugar estate there be a demand therefore for laborer on the part of those who wish to become planter and this demand be supply not only from the coast of africa but from the american continent and southwestern asia in one of the afternoon of holy week i see amid the crowd on the _ plaza de armas _ in havana several man of low stature of a deep-olive complexion beardless with high cheek-bone and straight black hair dress in white pantaloons of cotton and shirt of the same material wear over they they be indian native of yucatan who have be take prisoner of war by the whites of the country and sell to white man in cuba under a pretended contract to serve for a certain number of year i afterward learn that the dealer in this sort of merchandise be also bring in the native of asia chinese they call they here though i doubt whether they belong to that nation and dispose of they service to the planter there be six hundred of these people i have be tell in this city yesterday appear in the havana papers a ordinance concern the indian and asiatic import into the country under a contract to labor it direct how much indian corn how many plantain how much jerked-pork and rice they shall receive daily and how many lash the master may inflict for misbehavior twelve stripe with the cowskin he may administer for the smaller offens and twenty-four for transgression of more importance but if any more become necessary he must apply to a magistrate for permission to lay they on such be the manner in which the government of cuba sanction the barbarity of make slave of the freeborn man of yucatan the ordinance however betray great concern for the salvation of the soul of those whom it thus deliver over to the lash of the slave-driver it speak of the indian from america as christian already but while it allow the slave import from asia to be flog it direct that they shall be carefully instruct in the doctrine of we holy religion yet the policy of the government favor emancipation the law of cuba permit any slave to purchase he freedom on pay a price fix by three person one appoint by he master and two by a magistrate he may also if he please compel he master to sell he a certain portion of he time which he may employ to earn the means of purchase he entire freedom it be owe to this i suppose that the number of free black be so large in the island and it be manifest that if the slave-trade could be check and these law remain unaltered the negro would gradually emancipate themselves -- all at least who would be worth keep as servant the population of cuba be now about a million and a quarter rather more than half of whom be color person and one out of every four of the color population be free the mulatto emancipate themselves as a matter of course and some of they become rich by the occupation they follow the prejudice of color be by no means so strong here as in the united state five or six year since the negro be shout and bet in the cockpit with the whites but since the mulatto insurrection as it be call in 1843 the law forbid they presence at such amusement i be tell there be little difficulty in smuggling people of mixed blood by the help of legal form into the white race and if they be rich into good society provide they hair be not frizzle you hear something say now and then in the united state concern the annexation of cuba to we confederacy you may be curious perhaps to know what they say of it here a european who have long reside in the island give I this account : the creole no doubt would be very glad to see cuba annex to the united state and many of they ardently desire it it would relieve they from many great burden they now bear open they commerce to the world rid they of a tyrannical government and allow they to manage they own affair in they own way but spain derive from the possession of cuba advantage too great to be relinquish she extract from cuba a revenue of twelve million of dollar she government send its needy nobility and all for whom it would provide to fill lucrative office in cuba -- the priest the military officer the civil authority every man who fill a judicial post or hold a clerkship be from old spain the spanish government dare not give up cuba if it be incline nor will the people of cuba make any effort to emancipate themselves by take up arm the struggle with the power of spain would be bloody and uncertain even if the white population be unite but the mutual distrust with which the planter and the peasantry regard each other would make the issue of such a enterprise still more doubtful at present it would not be safe for a cuba planter to speak publicly of annexation to the united state he would run the risk of be imprison or exile of course if cuba be to be annex to the united state the slave trade with africa would cease to be carry on as now though its perfect suppression might be find difficult negro would be import in large number from the united state and planter would emigrate with they institution of education would be introduce commerce and religion would both be make free and the character of the islander would be elevate by the responsibility which a free government would throw upon they the planter however would doubtless adopt regulation insure the perpetuity of slavery they would unquestionably as soon as they be allow to frame ordinance for the island take away the facility which the present law give the slave for effect he own emancipation letter l english exhibition of work of art london _ July _ 7 1849 i have just be to which appear to I the most remarkable though not in the highest department of art be still-life piece by hunt it seem to I impossible to carry pictorial illusion to a higher pitch than he have attain plan of form in time a various and valuable collection of the work of british artist the greatest work of etty be the series represent the death of holoferne by the hand of judith it consist of three painting the first of which show judith in prayer before the execution of she attempt in the next and the finest she be see standing by the conch of the heathen warrior with the sword raise to heaven who stand on guard at the entrance and exhibit to she astonished handmaid who have be wait the result the subject be a old one but etty have treat it in a new way and give it a moral interest which that of ruben he reputation for these quality have influence he choice of subject in a remarkable manner the wall of the exhibition be cover writh venus and eve cupid and psyche and nymph innocent of the exhibition it represent the chaplain of the duke leave the table in a rage after a harangue by don quixote in praise of knight-errantry the suppress mirth of the duke and duchess the sly look of the servant the stormy anger of the ecclesiastic and the serene gravity of the knight be well express but there be a stiffness in some of the figure which make they look as if copy from the wooden model in the artist 's study and a raw and crude appearance in the handle so that you be remind of the brush every time you look at the painting to do leslie justice however he painting ripen wonderfully and seem to acquire a finish with year if one wish to form a idea of the vast number of indifferent painting which be annually produce in england he should visit as i do another exhibition a large gallery light from above in which each artist most of they of the younger or obscurer class take a certain number of foot on the wall and exhibit just what he please every man be he own hanging committee and if he picture be not place in the most advantageous position it be he own fault here acre of canvas be exhibit most of which be spoil of course though here and there a good picture be to be see and other which give promise of future merit enough of picture the principal subject of political discussion since i have be in england have be the expediency of allow jew to sit in parliament you have see by what a large majority baron rothschild have be again return from the city of london after he resignation in spite of the zealous opposition of the conservative it be allow i think on all hand that the majority of the nation be in favor of allow jew to hold seat in parliament but the other side urge the inconsistency of maintain a christian church as a state institution and admit the enemy of christianity to a share in its administration public opinion however be so strongly against political disability on account of religious faith that with the aid of the ministry it will no doubt triumph and we shall see another class of adversary of the establishment make war upon it in the house of common nor will it be at all surprising if after a little while we hear of jewish baron earl and marquis in the house of peer rothschild himself may become the founder of a noble line opulent beyond the proudest of they all the protectionist party here be labor to persuade the people that the government have commit a great error in grant such liberal condition to the trade of other nation to the prejudice of british industry they do not however seem to make much impression on the public mind the necessaries of life be obtain at a cheaper rate than formerly and that satisfy the people peel have be make a speech in parliament on the free-trade question which i often hear refer to as a very able argument for the free-trade policy neither on this question nor on that of the jewish disability do the opposition seem to have the country with they letter li a visit to the shetland isle aberdeen _ July _ 19 1849 two day ago i be in the orkney the day before i be in the shetland isle the farthest thule of the roman where i climb the noup of the noss as the famous headland of the island of noss be call from which you look out upon the sea that lie between shetland and norway from wick a considerable fishing town in caithness on the northern coast of scotland a steamer name the queen depart once a week in the summer month for kirkwall in the orkney and lerwick in shetland we go on board of she about ten o'clock on the 14th of July the herr fishery have just begin and the artificial port of wick construct with massive wall of stone be crowd with fishing vessel which have return that morning from the labor of the night for in the herr fishery it be only in the night that the net be spread and draw many of the vessel have land they cargo in other the fisherman be busily disengage the herring from the black net and throw they in heap and now and then a boat later than the rest be enter from the sea the green height all around the bay be cover with group of woman sit or walk dress for the most part in cap and white short gown wait for the arrival of the boat man by they husband and brother or belong to the family of those who have come to seek occupation as fisherman i have see two or three of the principal street of wick that morning swarm with strap fellow in blue highland bonnet with blue jacket and pantaloons and coarse blue flannel shirt a shopkeeper standing at he door instruct I who they be they be man of the celtic race he say -- the term celtic have grow to be quite fashionable i find when apply to the highlander they come from the hebride and other part of western scotland to get employment in the herr fishery these people have travel perhaps three hundred mile most of they on foot to be employ six or seven week for which they will receive about six pound wages those whom you see be not the best of they class the more enterprising and industrious have boat of they own and carry on the fishery on they own account we find the queen a strong steamboat with a good cabin and convenient state-room but dirty and smell of fish from stem to stern it have seem to I that the further north i go the more dirt i find we captain be a old aberdeen seaman with a stoop in he shoulder and look as if he be continually watch for land a occupation for which the foggy climate of these latitudes give he full scope we leave wick between eleven and twelve o'clock in the forenoon and glide over a calm sea with a cloudless sky above we and a thin haze on the surface of the water the haze thicken to a fog which grow more and more dense and finally close overhead after about three hour sail the captain begin to grow uneasy and be see walk about on the bridge between the wheel-houses anxiously peer into the mist on the look-out for the coast of the orkney at length he give up the search and stop the engine the passenger amuse themselves with fishing several coal-fish a large fish of slender shape be catch and one fine cod be haul up by a gentleman who unite in he person as he give I to understand the two capacity of portrait-painter and preacher of the gospel and who hold that the universal church of christendom have go sadly astray from the true primitive doctrine in regard to the time when the millennium be to take place the fog clear away in the evening we steamer be again in motion : we land at kirkwall in the middle of the night and when i go on deck the next morning we be smoothly pass the shore of fair isle -- high and steep rock impend over the water with a covering of green turf before they be out of sight we see the shetland coast the dark rock of sumburgh head and behind it half shroud in mist the promontory of fitfiel head -- fitful head as it be call by scott in he novel of the pirate beyond to the east black rocky promontory come in sight one after the other beetle over the sea at ten o'clock we be pass through a channel between the island lead to lerwick the capital of shetland on the principal island bear the name of mainland field yellow with flower among which stand here and there a cottage slope softly down to the water and beyond they rise the bare declivity and summit of the hill dark with heath with here and there still darker spot of a almost inky hue where peat have be cut for fuel not a tree not a shrub be to be see and the greater part of the soil appear never to have be reduce to cultivation about one o'clock we cast anchor before lerwick a fishing village build on the shore of bressay sound which here form one of the finest harbor in the world it have two passage to the sea so that when the wind blow a storm on one side of the island the shetlander in he boat pass out in the other direction and find himself in comparatively smooth water it be Sunday and the man who land we at the quay and take we baggage to we lodging say as he leave we -- it be the sabbath and i 'll no tak ' my pay now but i 'll call the morrow my name be jim sinclair pilot and if ye 'll be want to go anywhere i 'll be glad to tak ' ye in my boat in a few minute we be snugly establish at we lodgings there be no inn throughout all the shetland island which contain about thirty thousand inhabitant but if any of my friend should have occasion to visit lerwick i can cheerfully recommend to they the comfortable lodging-house of mr walker who keep a little shop in the principal street not far from queen 's lane we make haste to get ready for church and sally out to find the place of worship frequent by we landlady which be not a difficult matter the little town of lerwick consist of two-story house build mostly of unhewn stone rough-cast with steep roof and a chimney at each end they be arrange along a wind street parallel with the shore and along narrow lane run upward to the top of the hill the main street be flag with smooth stone like the street in venice for no vehicle run on wheel in the shetland island we go up queen 's lane and soon find the building occupy by the free church of scotland until a temple of fairer proportion on which the mason be now at work on the top of the hill shall be complete for they reception it be crowd with attentive worshiper one of whom obligingly come forward and find a seat for we the minister mr frazer have begin the evening service and be at prayer when i enter he be speak of we father the devil but the prayer be follow by a earnest practical discourse though somewhat crude in the composition and remind I of a expression i once hear use by a distinguished scotchman who complain that the clergy of he country in compose they sermon too often mak ' rough wark of it i look about among these descendant of the norwegian but could not see any thing singular in they physiognomy and but for the harsh accent of the preacher i might almost have think myself in the midst of a country congregation in the united state they be mostly of a light complexion with a appearance of health and strength though of a sparer make than the people of the more southern british isle after the service be over we return to we lodgings by a way which lead to the top of the hill and make the circuit of the little town the path lead into the interior of the island be full of people return homeward the woman in they best attire a few in silk with wind-tanned face we see they disappear one after another in the hollow or over the dark bare hill-top with a population of less than three thousand soul lerwick have four place of worship -- a church of the establishment a free church a church for the seceder and one for the methodist the road we take command a fine view of the harbor surround and sheltered by hill within it lay a numerous group of idle fishing-vessel with one great steamer in the midst and more formidable in appearance a dutch man-of-war send to protect the dutch fishery with the flag of holland fly at the mast-head above the town on tall pole be float the flag of four or five different nation to mark the habitation of they consul on the side opposite to the harbor lay the small fresh-water lake of cleikimin with the remains of a pictish castle in the midst one of those circular building of unhewn uncemented stone skillfully lay form apartment and gallery of such small dimension as to lead sir walter scott to infer that the pict be a people of a stature considerably below the ordinary standard of the human race a deep sabbath silence reign over the scene except the sound of the wind which here never cease to blow from one quarter or another as it sweep the herbage and beat against the stone wall surround the field the ground under we foot be thick with daisy and the blossom of the crow-foot and other flower for in the brief summer of these island nature which have no grove to embellish make amends by prank the ground particularly in the uncultivated part with a great profusion and variety of flower the next morning we be row by two of jim sinclair 's boy to the island of bressay and one of they act as we guide to the remarkable precipice call the noup of the noss we ascend its smooth slope and pasture and pass through one or two hamlet where we observe the construction of the dwelling of the zetland peasantry they be build of unhewn stone with roof of turf hold down by rope of straw neatly twist the floor be of earth the cow pony and pig live under the same roof with the family and the manure pond a receptacle for refuse and filth be close to the door a little higher up we come upon the uncultivated grounds abandon to heath and only use to supply fuel by the cutting of peat here and there woman be busy pile the square piece of peat in stack that they might dry in the wind we carry home these pit in a basket on we showlder when they be dry say one of they to I but those who can afford to keep a pony make he do this work for they in the hollow of this part of the island we see several fresh-water pond which be enlarged with dyke and make to turn grist mill we peep into one or two of these mill little stone building in which we could hardly stand upright inclose two small stone turn by a perpendicular shaft in which be half a dozen cog the paddle be fix below and there strike by the water turn the upper stone a steep descent bring we to the little strait border with rock which divide brassey from the island call the noss a strong south wind be drive in the billow from the sea with noise and foam but they be break and check by a bar of rock in the middle of the strait and we cross to the north of it in smooth water the ferryman tell we that when the wind be northerly he cross to the south of the bar as we climb the hill of the noss the mist begin to drift thinly around we from the sea and flock of sea-bird rise scream from the ground at we approach at length we stand upon the brink of a precipice of fearful height from which we have a full view of the still higher precipice of the neighbor summit a wall of rock be before we six hundred foot in height descend almost perpendicularly to the sea which roar and foam at its base among huge mass of rock and plunge into great cavern hollow out by the beating of the surge for century midway on the rock and above the reach of the spray be thousand of sea-bird sit in rank on the numerous shelf or alighting or take wing and scream as they fly a cloud of they be constantly in the air in front of the rock and over we head here they make they nest and rear they young but not entirely safe from the pursuit of the zetlander who cause himself to be let down by a rope from the summit and plunder they nest the face of the rock above the portion which be the haunt of the bird be fairly tapestried with herbage and flower which the perpetual moisture of the atmosphere keep always fresh -- daisy nod in the wind and the crimson phlox seem to set the cliff on flame yellow buttercup and a variety of other plant in bloom of which i do not know the name magnificent as this spectacle be we be not satisfy without climb to the summit as we pass upward we see where the rabbit have make they burrow in the elastic peat-like soil close to the very edge of the precipice we now find ourselves involve in the cold stream of mist which the strong sea-wind be drift over we they be in fact the lower skirt of the cloud at time they would clear away and give we a prospect of the green island summit around we with they bold headland the wind strait between and the black rock stand out in the sea when we arrive at the summit we could hardly stand against the wind but it be almost more difficult to muster courage to look down that dizzy depth over which the zetlander suspend themselves with rope in quest of the egg of the sea-fowl my friend capture a young gull on the summit of the noup the bird have rise at he approach and essay to fly towards the sea but the strength of the wind drive he back to the land he rise again but could not sustain a long flight and come to the ground again be catch after a spirited chase amidst a wild clamor of of the sea-fowl over we head not far from the noup be the holm or as it be sometimes call the cradle or basket of the noss it be a perpendicular mass of rock two or three hundred foot high with a broad flat summit richly cover with grass and be separate from the island by a narrow chasm through which the sea flow two strong rope be stretch from the main island to the top of the holm and on these be sling the cradle or basket a sort of open box make of deal board in which the shepherd pass with they sheep to the top of the holm we find the cradle strongly secure by lock and key to the stake on the side of the noss in order no doubt to prevent any person from cross for he own amusement as we descend the smooth pasture of the noss we fall in with a herd of pony of a size somewhat larger than be common on the island i ask we guide a lad of fourteen year of age what be the average price of a sheltie he answer deserve to be write in letter of gold -- it be jist as they be bug a ' smal ' from the ferryman at the strait below i get more specific information they vary in price from three to ten pound but the latter sum be only pay for the finest of these animal in the respects of shape and color it be not a little remarkable that the same cause which in shetland have make the horse the smallest of pony have almost equally reduce the size of the cow the sheep also -- a pretty creature i might call it -- from the fine wool of which the shetland woman knot the thin web know by the name of shetland shawl be much smaller than any breed i have ever see whether the cause be the perpetual chilliness of the atmosphere or the insufficiency of nourishment -- for though the long zetland winter be temperate and snow never lie long on the ground there be scarce any growth of herbage in that season -- i will not undertake to say but the people of the island ascribe it to the insufficiency of nourishment it be at all event remarkable that the tradition of the country should ascribe to the pict the early inhabitant of shetland the same dwarfish stature and that the numerous remains of they habitation which still exist should seem to confirm the tradition the race which at present possess the shetland be however of what the french call a advantageous stature and well limb if it be the want of a proper and genial warmth which prevent the due growth of the domestic animal it be a want to which the zetlander be not subject they hill afford the man apparently inexhaustible supply of peat which cost the poorest man nothing but the trouble of cut it and bring it home and they cottage i be tell be always well warm in winter in cross the narrow strait which separate the noss from bressay i observe on the bressay side overlook the water a round hillock of very regular shape in which the green turf be intermix with stone that say the ferryman be what we call a pictish castle i mind when it be open it be full of room so that ye could go over every part of it i climb these opening reveal have be completely choke up in take away the material of which they be build although plenty of stone may be find everywhere in the island there seem to be a disposition to overhead pour out they sweet note so fast and so ceaselessly that it seem as if the little creature imagine they have more to utter than they have time to utter it in in no part of the british island have i see the lark so numerous or so merry as in the shetland we wait awhile at the wharf by the minister 's house in bressay for jim sinclair who at length appear in he boat to convey we to lerwick the city be then in what be call by a convenient fiction a state of siege soldier fill the street be post in every public square and at every corner be see march before the church the cornice of which demonstration of fraternity less formal but the show and the menace of military force be scarcely less apparent those who maintain that france be not fit for liberty need not afflict themseve with the arrest and imprison at pleasure he may be guiltless of any offense which could be make a pretext for condemn he but he trial be arbitrarily postpone and when at last he be release he have suffer the scythe in all the village through which we pass in the very smallest troops be post and man in military uniform be stand at the door or look from the window of every inn and beer-house at heilbronn we and we enter stuttgart with a little army that city too look as if in a state of siege so numerous be the soldiery though the vine-covered hill among which it be situate could have give they a better the goldener hirsch say to my friend i think i shall emigrate to america i be tire of live under the bayonet i be in munich when the news arrive of the surrender of the hungarian troops under görgey war lead on by they little officer in regular military order march and wheeling to the sound of martial music with all the precision of veteran in switzerland arm be in every man 's hand he be educate target-shooting _ freischiessen _ be the national amusement of switzerland and have be so ever since the day of tell occasion of target-shooting be prescribe and superintend by the public authority have be announce that the french government require of these poor fellow that they should either enlist at once in the regiment destine for service in algier or immediately leave the country -- offer occupy in the late remodelling of european institution seem to have think of abolish this invention of despotism -- this restraint upon the liberty of pass from place to place which make europe one peaceably perfect they liberty whenever they be enlighten enough to claim they on my way from geneva to lyon i sit in _ banquette _ of the diligence among the plebeian the conversation happen to turn
